401 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Allegheny Clarklift v. Woodline Indus. of Pa.


No. 01032 Pittsburgh 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


356 Pa. Super. 269; 514 A.2d 606; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 12130


April 30, 1986, Argued  
August 29, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Civil, at No. C.P. 85-1055, Book 188 Page 360.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee forklift owner summary judgment in appellee's replevin action to recover its forklift that had been distrained by appellant pursuant to the Landlord and Tenant Act, 68 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 250.302, et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellee forklift owner brought an action for replevin against appellant landlord. Appellee filed a motion for summary judgment. The lower court granted the motion. The court affirmed the summary judgment. The court held that the constitutional question was properly before it because it was raised in the lower court and did not disappear though the trial court circumvented it. The court held that the distraint provisions of the Landlord and Tenant Act, 68 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 250.302, et seq., were unconstitutional because they violated due process protections under the U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that once the statute was invalidated, the exemplary behavior of appellant was irrelevant. The court found that appellee was not even the delinquent tenant against whom the forklift was seized.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment against appellant landlord because the distraint provisions of the Landlord and Tenant Act (act) unconstitutionally deprived appellee forklift owner of due process. The act allowed appellant, without notice or other procedural safeguard, to seize appellee's personal property that was located on the premises that were leased to another party.

 

402 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Arnold


No. 1602 Pittsburgh, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


356 Pa. Super. 343; 514 A.2d 890; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 12162


June 3, 1986, Argued  
August 28, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County, Criminal Division, Nos. 827, 827 3/6, 827 4/6, 827 5/6 of 1984.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of corrupting the morals of a minor, indecent assault, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, and indecent exposure, and imposed a prison term under the provisions of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9718. Defendant was also ordered to pay prosecution costs, a fine, and a sum to the Crime Victims Compensation fund.


OVERVIEW: Defendant made statements to a children and youth services caseworker about sexual conduct with his live-in girlfriend's six-year-old child, and the caseworker reported it to the police. Upon arrest, defendant made inculpatory statements to the police after being read his Miranda warnings. Defendant was convicted by the trial court of sexual and other offenses involving a minor. Defendant was fined, ordered to pay prosecution costs, and sentenced under the Pennsylvania Mandatory Sentencing Statute. Defendant's motions for certified appeal, in arrest of judgment, for a new trial, to suppress his statements, and to declare the reporting provision of the Child Protective Services Act (Act), 11 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2201 et seq., unconstitutional were denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The statements to the caseworker and the police were constitutional because they were made without compulsion. The court held that the Act was constitutional and that there is no privacy right for criminal acts in the home and, thus, no Miranda warning was needed to speak to the caseworker. The court found the mandatory sentencing statute was not unconstitutional as cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for sexual offenses with a minor. The imposition of a mandatory sentence under the Pennsylvania Mandatory Sentencing Statute was not unconstitutional cruel and unusual punishment. The court held that the reporting provision of the Pennsylvania Child Protective Services Act is not unconstitutional because there was no fundamental right to privacy for crimes in the home.

 

403 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Kornicki


No. 244 Philadelphia 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


357 Pa. Super. 182; 515 A.2d 925; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 11915


May 14, 1986, Argued  
August 27, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 20, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Bucks County at No. 2094, 3582 of 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which denied its motion to modify and/or resentence. The commonwealth averred that the trial court erred in not considering prior driving under the influence convictions in computing defendant's sentence under the statutorily mandated minimums of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence (DUI), in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §3731(a)(1). In determining sentence, the commonwealth offered to the sentencing judge a total of four DUI convictions, two of which occurred prior to the effective date of the new recidivist provisions of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(e). On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for proper sentencing, finding that the sentencing court was required to consider convictions that occurred before the new law went into effect in imposing sentence on a subsequent conviction. The court had previously held § 3731(e) to be constitutional because it did not transform a previously legal act into an unlawful one, it did not violate the constitutional provisions proscribing ex post facto laws, and did not permit the sentence attending the previous conviction to be increased. It merely notified an offender of what punishment would attend convictions based upon post-amendment violations. The court therefore held that commonwealth was entitled to pursue an enhancement of the sentence.


OUTCOME: The denial of the commonwealth's motion to modify and/or resentence was reversed and the conviction remanded for resentencing, because defendant's driving under the influence convictions, which occurred before enactment of statutory provisions for enhanced sentencing, should have been considered where the recidivist provisions were previously held constitutional.

 

404 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Mortimer


No. 1280 Pittsburgh, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


356 Pa. Super. 262; 514 A.2d 602; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 12150


January 21, 1986, Submitted  
August 25, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Mercer County at No. 173 Criminal, 1985.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review after the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County (Pennsylvania) used an uncounseled summary offense to enhance a subsequent summary offense to a misdemeanor, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 106(c)(2), 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3929(b)(1)(ii), Pa. R. Crim. P. 316(a)(c)(i).


OVERVIEW: In 1980, appellant pled guilty to retail theft, a summary violation, for which appellant was fined $ 100 and assessed $ 26 costs, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 106(c)(2). Appellant was not represented. In 1985, appellant again pled guilty to retail theft. The trial court considered the prior conviction and enhanced the grading of the current offense from a summary violation to a second-degree misdemeanor, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3929(b)(1)(ii). After the trial court denied his motion to modify the sentence, appellant sought review. The court affirmed. The court held that there was no right to counsel for all summary convictions, under Pa. R. Crim. P. 316(a)(c)(i). The court held that appellant had no right to counsel for his first summary conviction because appellant was not imprisoned. The court held that the trial court properly used appellant's first summary conviction to enhance his second summary conviction because appellant's subsequent offense did not create a right to counsel, where none otherwise existed, for the summary offense of retail theft.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's use of appellant's uncounseled summary offense conviction to enhance his subsequent offense to a misdemeanor. The court held that appellant had no right to counsel for his first summary offense because appellant was not imprisoned. The court held that the validity of appellant's first conviction was not impugned when the trial court used that conviction to enhance a subsequent conviction to a misdemeanor.

 

405 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Stinnett


No. 1903 Philadelphia, 1982


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


356 Pa. Super. 83; 514 A.2d 154; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 11830


February 10, 1986, Submitted  
August 19, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence entered June 17, 1982 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County at Nos. 2795, 2797 of 1981. CRIMINAL SECTION



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, Judgment of Sentence is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of sentence entered on his conviction for robbery in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentence on his conviction of robbery. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment holding that appellant failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he did not disclose what evidence counsel failed to discover and how it would have aided his cause and the claims counsel failed to pursue were meritless. The court also held that the trial court properly permitted admission of appellant's prior conviction because he held a Bighum hearing. The court further held that appellant had no substantive right in the less aggressive prosecutorial effort which is commonly afforded criminal defendants not prosecuted under career criminal programs and that his claims of denial of equal protection and due process were without factual or legal support. Lastly, the court held that the trial court properly restricted the cross-examination of police witnesses because the questions were directed to matters which were not related to the incident at issue or the parties involved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence on appellant's conviction of robbery. The court held that counsel provided effective assistance, that the trial judge did not abuse his discretion by permitting appellant's prior convictions to be used to impeach him, that appellants due process rights were not violated, and that the trial court did not improperly limit cross-examination of police witnesses.

 

406 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Stenhach


Nos. 00242 Pittsburgh, 1985, 00244 Pittsburgh, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


356 Pa. Super. 5; 514 A.2d 114; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 11717


October 29, 1985, Argued  
July 31, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of February 8, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Potter County, Criminal Division, at No. 85 of 1983 (No. 00242 Pittsburgh, 1985).

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of February 8, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Potter County, Criminal Division, at No. 84 of 1983 (No. 00244 Pittsburgh, 1985).



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgments of sentence are vacated and appellants discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant defense attorneys challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Potter County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced them to prison following their convictions of hindering prosecution, tampering, criminal solicitation, and criminal conspiracy. Appellants contended that they properly retained physical evidence in a murder trial, citing principles of attorney-client privilege, constitutional protection, and professional responsibility.


OVERVIEW: Appellant defense attorneys were sentenced to prison for convictions of hindering prosecution, tampering, criminal solicitation, and criminal conspiracy, after they retained physical evidence in a murder trial. On appeal, the court vacated the judgments of sentence and discharged the appellants. The court held that the statutes against tampering with physical evidence 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4910, and hindering prosecution, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5101, were unconstitutionally overbroad when applied to appellants representing criminal defendants. The court also held that physical evidence of crime in the possession of a criminal defense attorney was not subject to a privilege but had to be delivered to the prosecution. Additionally, the court held that the state's seizure of evidence in the possession of the attorney did not violate the defendant's rights, and it was an abuse of a lawyer's professional responsibility knowingly to take possession of and secrete the instrumentalities of a crime. The court announced the rule that a defense attorney must, after a reasonable time, turn evidence taken from its original resting place over to the prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgments of sentence for appellants' convictions of hindering prosecution, tampering, criminal solicitation, and criminal solicitation and ordered them discharged. The statutes against tampering with physical evidence and hindering prosecution were unconstitutionally overbroad when applied to appellants. Physical evidence of crime in the possession of a criminal defense attorney was not privileged.

 

407 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Fetterhoff v. Fetterhoff


No. 644 Harrisburg, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


354 Pa. Super. 438; 512 A.2d 30; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 11356


May 20, 1986, Submitted  
July 7, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Civil Division, No. 2462 S 1984



DISPOSITION:                               JUDGMENT

ON CONSIDERATION WHEREOF, it is now hereby ordered and adjudged by this Court that the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant consumer sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee designer-installer in a personal injury action filed by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellee designer-installer constructed an elevator shaft in appellant consumer's home. Appellant filed a personal injury action nearly 30 years after the construction was completed. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of appellee finding that the cause of action was barred under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 5536, which provided for a 12-year statute of repose. Upon review, the court affirmed. The court rejected appellant's argument that appellee was not entitled to the protection of the repose statute because appellee performed periodic repair work. The court reasoned that appellee never had actual possession or control of the elevator as owner, tenant or otherwise where appellee only performed maintenance and repair work when summoned by appellant's family. The court found that appellee, as designer, manufacturer, and installer of the elevator unquestionably fell under the scope of the repose statute and appellant's claim was time-barred. The court held the date of delivery of the improvement fixed the date actuating the repose statute and the periodic maintenance did not revitalize the limitations period.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order that granted summary judgment in favor of appellee designer-installer in a personal injury action filed by appellant consumer. The court held that appellee was protected by a repose statute of limitations where appellee did not have a duty to maintain an elevator and did not exercise control or ownership of the elevator.

 

408 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Thier


No. 00885 Philadelphia 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


354 Pa. Super. 7; 510 A.2d 1251; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 11048


April 1, 1986, Argued  
June 16, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order entered February 28, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal No. 461, 461A, B, C, D of 1984.



DISPOSITION: Having found no merit in appellant's ten points of error, we affirm the decision of the lower court. Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) convicting him for five violations of 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1272, regarding the illegal sale of fireworks, and for a minimum sentence of 60 days in prison and a fine. Appellant defended himself both at trial and on appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of five violations of 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1272, regarding the illegal sale of fireworks. Appellant challenged the trial court's judgment and the court affirmed the judgment and determined that there was no merit to appellant's 10 points of error. The court held that it would not reach appellant's first six points of error because they were not properly preserved for review. The court also held that points of error alleging an improper charge to the jury were baseless because both of the charges claimed to have been omitted were included in the charge. The court rejected the argument that the trial court did not have jurisdiction because the facts clearly showed appellant delivered illegal fireworks in the county in which he was charged. The court stated that absent a knowing and intelligent waiver, a person could not be imprisoned for any offense, whether classified as petty, misdemeanor, or felony, unless he or she was represented by counsel. The court held that appellant's waiver of his right to counsel subsequent to the explanation of rights and possible consequences of self representation by trial judge was a knowing and intelligent waiver of such right.


OUTCOME: The court held that there was no merit to any of appellant's 10 points of error and affirmed the trial court's order convicting appellant of selling illegal fireworks. Appellant made a knowing and intelligent waiver of his right to counsel, and thus, appellant's rights were not violated.

 

409 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hill


No. 00586 Harrisburg 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


354 Pa. Super. 32; 511 A.2d 171; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10933


January 16, 1986, Submitted  
June 4, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 18, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Dauphin County, at No. 1509-1510 C.D. 1972.



DISPOSITION: Order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which entered an order that dismissed, without a hearing, a petition filed under the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1180-3, which had been filed by defendant.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of two counts of murder in the second degree. A direct appeal to the state supreme court followed, and the judgments of sentence were affirmed. When the supreme court received a second petition for review, it transferred the matter to a special superior court panel, which affirmed the judgment of sentence. Defendant's petition for allowance of appeal was granted, and the supreme court again affirmed the judgment of sentence. Defendant instituted habeas corpus proceedings in the federal district court, which denied defendant relief. On appeal, the court directed that the appeal should have been considered timely filed. The court granted defendant's petition for reconsideration. The court had no difficulty in concluding with substantial assurance that malice was more likely than not to flow from the proof of an unlawful killing without justification or excuse, where the act of slaying was voluntary. The court held that defense counsel was not ineffective for failing to advance a claim which defendant did not have the burden to prove. The order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of defendant's post-conviction relief hearing after he was convicted of murder in the second degree, because defense counsel was not ineffective for failing to advance a claim which defendant did not have the burden to prove, and because the state had proved that malice was more likely than not to flow from the proof of a voluntary unlawful killing without justification.

Commonwealth v. Kopycinski


No. 00259 Pittsburg, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 387; 510 A.2d 365; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10993


November 25, 1985, Submitted  
June 4, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted October 30, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of January 30, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. CC 8310151A OTN: B-186377-2.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and case remanded for re-sentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Alleghany County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellee of driving while under the influence of alcohol and assessing a punishment of two days' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed and remanded for re-sentencing the decision of the trial court. Appellee was convicted for driving while under the influence of alcohol and assessed a punishment to serve two days in jail, pay a $ 300 fine, undergo evaluation by an alcohol program, and attend a safe driving school. The trial court refused to apply a recidivist statute, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(e)(1)(ii), requiring a mandatory 30 day sentence for a second offense. The trial court reasoned that it would be violative of the constitutional provisions proscribing ex post facto laws as the previous conviction occurred before the 30 day sentence requirement was in effect. On appeal, the court held that an application of the recidivist statute was constitutional as the statute's mandatory sentence did not transform a legal act into an unlawful one. The court also held that the commonwealth was not required to allege prior convictions in a criminal information.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment for resentencing on the grounds that application of the recidivist statute was not violative of constitutional provisions proscribing ex post fact laws.

Mihalcik v. Celotex Corp.

Appeal from the Order entered January 10, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 80-19444.

Appeal from the Order entered January 10, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 81-06213.

Appeal from the Order entered January 10, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 81-07572.

Appeal from the Order entered January 10, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 81-10376.

Appeal from the Order entered January 10, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 80-20569.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing [***2] reasons, we affirm the orders of the lower court on appeals Nos. 3372 and 3373 PHL 84 and Nos. 245, 248-250, and 252 PHL 85; reverse the lower court's dismissal of the survival actions in tort and breach of warranty in Nos. 246 and 247 PHL 85, and its dismissal of the wrongful death action in No. 247 PHL 85; and remand for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction is not retained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant surviving spouse sought review of the decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which, in consolidated appeals, granted appellee asbestos companies' motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant surviving spouse sought review of trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee asbestos companies. This case consolidated appeals from the orders of the lower court granting appellees' motions for summary judgment. In each case, an appellant brought a wrongful death and survival action grounded in negligence, strict liability in tort, and breach of warranty against appellees, asbestos companies, for asbestos-related injuries suffered by a decedent. Appellees filed motions for summary judgment, which were granted, by the trial court. On appeal, the court held that the discovery rule operated to toll the new statute of limitations for wrongful death and tort survival actions. The court reversed the order of the trial court as it granted summary judgment to appellees and dismissed the tort survival action, the wrongful death and tort survival claims. The court affirmed the orders of the trial court in part and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee asbestos companies in part and reversed the trial court's dismissal of the survival actions in tort and breach of warranty, and wrongful death action and remanded for further proceedings in part because the discovery rule operated to toll the new statute of limitations for wrongful death and tort survival actions.

 

412 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Maggio


Nos. 00895 Pittsburgh, 1985, 01385 Pittsburgh, 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 157; 509 A.2d 383; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10878


March 18, 1986, Argued  
May 14, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of June 19, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. 677 Misc. July, 1980.

Appeal from the Order Entered on October 24, 1983, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. 677 Misc. July, 1980.
Commonwealth v. Maggio, 37 Pa. D. & C.3d 527, 1983 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 49 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, whose charges were suspended based on incompetence to stand trial, challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which entered a mental health commitment pursuant to 50 P.S. § 4408.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, facing criminal charges suspended on account of incompetence to stand trial, challenged the lower court's mental health commitment, made pursuant to 50 P.S. § 4408. He claimed the commitment violated his due process rights. The court remanded to case for a new commitment hearing based on a new petition and appropriate notice under the applicable statute in order to permit preparation of a proper defense. The court held that had appellant been afforded the procedural protections of the Mental Health Procedures Act, 50 P.S. § 7102, prior to the hearing, evidence might have been different and resulted in commitment to a less restrictive facility. Appellant received no notice of the hearing, nor was he served with a petition, with the result that he had no opportunity to prepare a defense, thus his substantial right of due process was violated. The court held that it was error to commit appellant, suffering only from mental retardation, to a forensic unit designed and equipped for the treatment of the mentally ill. Thus, he had to be released or given the customary civil commitment proceeding that would be required to commit indefinitely any other citizen.


OUTCOME: The court held that appellant was entitled to a new commitment hearing based on a new petition and appropriate notice under the applicable statute in order to permit preparation of a proper defense. The commonwealth had to either release appellant or institute the customary civil commitment proceeding that would be required to commit indefinitely any other citizen as appellant was entitled to due process procedures.

 

413 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gallagher


No. 01726 PHL 84


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 426; 510 A.2d 735; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 12554


April 4, 1985, Argued  
May 12, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of May 29, 1984 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 82-09-2584.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment convicting him for involuntary deviate sexual intercourse under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3123.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. Defendant gained entrance to the victim's home by posing as a police officer, and sexually assaulted the complainant. The complainant was shown a photographic display containing defendant's picture, but was unable to identify him positively. Later, the complainant positively identified defendant in both a photographic display and a lineup. At trial, the jury found defendant guilty. On appeal, the court affirmed, and found no evidence that this testimony confused, misled, or prejudiced the jury. The court held that the testimony did not invade the province of the jury by impermissibly bolstering the complainant's credibility. Expert testimony provided by psychologists, who held doctoral rather than medical degrees, was admissible as to rape trauma syndrome, and was admissible to prove lack of consent or to corroborate testimony that a rape occurred. The court found that the lower court's desire to avoid a judge-shopping ploy was an adequate reason for denying defendant's request for a non-jury trial. The court held that defendant failed to meet his burden of proving counsel's ineffectiveness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for involuntary deviate sexual intercourse because the lower court's desire to avoid a judge-shopping ploy was an adequate reason for denying defendant's request for a non-jury trial, expert testimony was admissible as to rape trauma syndrome, and there was no abuse of discretion.

Maycock v. Gravely Corp.


NO. 00648 PITTSBURGH 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


352 Pa. Super. 421; 508 A.2d 330; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10607


January 30, 1986, Argued  
May 2, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 15, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of May 2, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Civil No. 3632-A-1983.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which granted a motion brought by appellees, company, father, and landowner, for a judgment on the pleadings in a personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured party challenged an order of the trial court which granted a motion brought by appellees, company, father, and landowner, for a judgment on the pleadings in appellant's personal injury action regarding injury caused by a lawnmower when appellant was a child. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that there was no clear and manifest intent by the general assembly that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5533(b) be applied retroactively. Appellant's claim was time barred because it was brought after appellant turned 18 years old and the injury occurred when appellant was a small child.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the granting of appellees', company, father, and landowner, action for a judgment on the pleadings in appellant injured party's action for personal injuries because the claim was time barred.

 

415 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Scott v. Adal Corp.


No. 0193 Philadelphia 1984; No. 2130 Philadelphia 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 288; 509 A.2d 1279; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10471


June 19, 1985, Argued  
May 1, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 16, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order Entered July 2, 1984, in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 4800 April Term, 1976.
Scott v. Adal Corp., 276 Pa. Super. 459, 419 A.2d 548, 1980 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2236 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Order reversed insofar as it concerns the unconstitutionality of Pa. R.C.P. 3129; affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtors sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellants' motion for summary judgment in part, holding that the notice requirements of the rules of civil procedure for sheriff's sales of real property did not comport with due process, but denying the motion in all other respects.


OVERVIEW: The action was commenced by a class action complaint in equity filed by appellant debtors on behalf of themselves and all others similarly situated. The complaint was filed against appellee creditor, collection agency, and sheriff which had exposed the appellants' real property to a sheriff's sale. The lower court granted appellee creditor and collection agency's preliminary objections and appellee sheriff's motion for judgment on the pleadings, dismissing the case. Appellants sought review and the court reversed that dismissal and remanded the case for a class action determination. The lower court granted appellants motion in part, holding that the notice requirements of the rules of civil procedure for sheriff's sales of real property did not comport with due process. The parties filed an appeal and a cross appeal. The court found that the appeal was properly before the court. The court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in failing to afford the requested equitable relief. The court found that there was no "taking" at the sheriff's sale in the constitutional sense because there had not been any appropriation for government use.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court insofar as it concerned the unconstitutionality of a rule of civil procedures, and affirmed in all other respects. The court held that the appellant debtors did not have a constitutionally protected right to be advised that if their property was sold for a grossly inadequate price, they could petition the court to have the sale set aside.

Commonwealth v. Michael


No. 01553 Philadelphia, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


352 Pa. Super. 345; 507 A.2d 1263; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10425


February 4, 1986, Submitted  
April 28, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of May 30, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal, No. 4821/1984



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced appellant to 5 to 10 years imprisonment after appellant pleaded guilty but mentally ill, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 314, to charges of robbery, receiving stolen property, simple assault, and related offenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant took the complainant's car and held a handgun on her. Appellant pleaded guilty but mentally ill, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 314, to charges of robbery, receiving stolen property, simple assault, and related offenses. Appellant was sentenced to 5 to 10 years imprisonment, and his motion for reconsideration of his sentence was denied by the lower court. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that imposed a 5 to 10 year sentence because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9727 was clear that when appellant pleaded guilty but mentally ill he could have received any sentence that could have lawfully been imposed on anyone convicted of the same offense. The court held that because an individual who was convicted of robbery and who was found to have visibility possessed a handgun, could have only been lawfully sentenced to an imprisonment of not less than five years, the same sentence applied to appellant. The court noted that other Pennsylvania courts previously rejected appellant's constitutional challenges to the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that sentenced appellant to 5 to 10 years imprisonment because the mandatory term of imprisonment of not less than five years for robbery with the visible possession of a handgun applied to appellant, who pleaded guilty but mentally ill. The court noted that other Pennsylvania courts previously rejected appellant's constitutional challenges to mandatory sentences.

Ross v. Bowlby


No. 00271 Pittsburgh, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 59; 509 A.2d 332; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10529


November 25, 1985, Submitted  
April 21, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered June 20, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Civil Division, at Nos. 139 Domestic, 1976 and 360 Domestic, 1984.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and the case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County (Pennsylvania), which found that a Maryland child support action was res judicata to the instant Pennsylvania child support action, in favor of appellee father.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother filed a child support action against appellee father. The action was transferred by a Revised Uniform Reciprocal Support Act Petition, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6741 et seq., to Maryland where appellee was living. A Maryland court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment based on Maryland's two- year statute of limitations in paternity actions. Subsequently, appellee moved back to Pennsylvania and appellant instituted another support action against appellee. The court reversed the order of the Pennsylvania lower court that found the child support decision of the Maryland court should be given res judicata effect, and held in favor of appellant mother because there was no final determination of paternity, and therefore, the doctrine of res judicata could not be invoked when a final judgment had not been rendered on the merits. The court held that a paternity action filed within six years of a child's birth was not barred by a dismissal based on the two year statute of limitations in effect when the paternity action was originally filed. The case was remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court that found that the dismissal of a child support action in a Maryland court should be given res judicata effect, because there was no final determination of paternity, and, therefore, the doctrine of res judicata could not be invoked. The court held that the retroactive application of Pennsylvania's six year statute of limitations could be applied to a paternity action by appellant mother.

In re Estate of Ogden


No. 00980 Pittsburgh 1985; No. 00981 Pittsburgh 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


353 Pa. Super. 273; 509 A.2d 1271; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10393


February 18, 1986, Argued  
April 18, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 25, 1986. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 12, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of June 25, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Orphans No. 627 of 1950.

Appeal from the Order of June 25, 1985, in the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Orphans No. 112 February Term 1951.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the lower court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant beneficiaries challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which held, in regard to the trustee's petition for distribution from decedent's trusts, that adoptees could not be excluded from distribution because they had been adopted into the class of beneficiaries and not born into it.


OVERVIEW: Appellant beneficiaries challenged the trial court's decision, in regard to the trustee's petition for distribution from the decedent's trusts, that adoptees could not be excluded from distribution on the grounds that they had been adopted into the class of beneficiaries and not born into it. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the language of the will and trust instruments was inconclusive, as evidenced by the inconsistent and interchangeable use of the terms "heirs of the body" and "children." The court held that absent the decedent's clear expression of contrary intent, adoptees were presumed to be included in the terms "children" or "issue," a presumption conforming with the statutory rule of will interpretation, 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2514(7) (1975, 1985), and public policy that the adopted child attained the status of a natural child of the adopting parents. The court held that the evidence established with reasonable certainty that it was not the decedent's intent to exclude adoptees, where she had an adopted great grandniece and -nephew when she died, made a bequest to one referencing his status as "adopted," and did not specifically exclude adoptees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision on trustee's petition for distribution, holding that adoptees were beneficiaries of decedent's will and trusts under the inconclusive terms "heirs of the body" and "children," where the terms were used inconsistently and interchangeably, state public policy and rules of interpretation favored that interpretation, and decedent, knowing of adopted relatives at her death, failed to specifically exclude adoptees.

Commonwealth v. Gamber

CASE SUMMARY:

The court held that the jury verdict was supported by evidence of the intoxilyzer result, testimony regarding defendant's physical condition or both.

Commonwealth v. Dougherty

351 Pa. Super. 603; 506 A.2d 936; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9881

OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol and sought review. Skinner v. Flymo, Inc.


No. 218 Pittsburgh, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


351 Pa. Super. 234; 505 A.2d 616; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9634


October 31, 1985, Argued  
February 21, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of the court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny county, No. GD 84-12804.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants husband and wife sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), finding that appellee trade association was not subject to the jurisdiction of the Pennsylvania courts in appellants' action for damages for personal injuries.


OVERVIEW: Appellants husband and wife brought an action for personal injuries, caused by a lawn mower, against five parties, including appellee trade association. The trial court sustained appellee's objection to jurisdiction, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, finding that appellee did not purposefully establish sufficient minimum contacts with Pennsylvania to subject it to specific jurisdiction because it had no activity whatsoever that was purposefully directed toward residents. The court found that the only connection between appellee's activity as a trade association and the commonwealth was the manufacturer's shipment of a lawn mower bearing appellee's label into Pennsylvania, and that this unilateral action by the manufacturer was insufficient to support jurisdiction over the trade association in Pennsylvania. The court found that, given the tenuous relationship between appellee's certification activities and appellant's injury, it was clear that appellee could not reasonably have anticipated being haled into court in Pennsylvania.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding of the trial court that appellee trade association did not purposefully establish sufficient minimum contacts with the commonwealth to subject it to specific jurisdiction in appellants husband and wife's action for personal injuries because it had no activity whatsoever directed towards residents.

Commonwealth v. Lark


No. 641 Philadelphia, 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


350 Pa. Super. 558; 504 A.2d 1291; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9478


September 11, 1985, Submitted  
February 12, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of March 4, 1985 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. 2986 August Term, 1983



DISPOSITION: Based on the foregoing, the judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), convicting him of third-degree murder and imposing a sentence of life imprisonment under the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9715, because of his prior second-degree murder conviction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of third-degree murder in the death of his infant daughter and sentenced to life imprisonment under the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9715, based on his prior conviction for second-degree murder. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed, finding that the trial court's general questions during voir dire were adequate to expose possible prejudice or bias even though the questions did not specifically address appellant's questions. The court found that the notice requirement of § 9715 was adequate and did not violate appellant's due process right to pretrial notice of the offense with which he was charged or its elements. The court found that the requirement that the prosecutor show that appellant had been convicted of a prior homicide by preponderant evidence was sufficient to protect appellant's liberty interest as weighed against the commonwealth's interest in imposing a mandatory sentence. The court found that appellant's equal protection rights were not violated by § 9715 because the classification for sentencing purposes was rationally related to the legitimate purpose of protecting general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for third degree murder, finding that the mandatory life sentence imposed did not violate appellant's due process rights because notice was adequate or his equal protection rights because classification for sentencing had legitimate purpose.

Commonwealth v. Gonzales


No. 2897 Philadelphia 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


350 Pa. Super. 373; 504 A.2d 886; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9436


March 4, 1985, Argued  
February 3, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted 11/18/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at Nos. 3170-3172 September 1982.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the Judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which increased his original sentence imposed on his conviction for third degree murder while in visible possession of a firearm. Appellant contended that the resentencing violated his double jeopardy rights, that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712 was unconstitutional, and that the evidence was insufficient.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court increased appellant's original sentence, he challenged the decision. The court affirmed. First, even though appellant had begun to serve his original sentence, the increased sentence did not impinge on his right to be protected from double jeopardy because appellant had not fully satisfied his sentence. Further, the Double Jeopardy Clause did not provide appellant with the right to perceive the length of his sentence as finally determined because pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712(d), the legislature specifically provided that the Commonwealth could appeal the sentence. Second, the court rejected appellant's contention that § 9712 was unconstitutional for altering the burden of proof and impermissibly delegating power to the executive, because the supreme court had recently upheld the constitutionality of § 9712, specifically rejecting those same issues. And finally, appellant's visible possession of a firearm was manifested in the process of the crime: by smoke, the sound of gunfire and a gunshot wound, even though the Commonwealth's witness never actually saw the gun.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence, which increased appellant's original sentence. The court held that because appellant had not satisfied his original sentence, his rights to double jeopardy were not violated, that the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act did not unconstitutionally alter the burden of proof or delegate power to the executive, and that appellant's possession was manifest by the process of the crime.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) for a guilty plea to the charge of driving under the influence of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentence entered pursuant to a guilty plea to a charge of driving under the influence of alcohol. The court found that appellant was provided such notice orally and that appellant made no objection or challenge to the validity of the conviction at that time. Appellant was also given a later opportunity to retract her plea.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence finding that the oral notice of the commonwealth's intent to enhance the sentence for prior violations of the drunk driving law, coupled with a later opportunity to for appellant to withdraw her guilty plea, was sufficient due process.

Commonwealth v. Potter


No. 00135 Philadelphia 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


350 Pa. Super. 61; 504 A.2d 243; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9273


September 12, 1985, Argued  
January 24, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE DECEMBER 13, 1984 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF DELAWARE COUNTY, CRIMINAL 6409-83.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of possession with intent to deliver drug paraphernalia in violation of 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(33) and sentenced him to three months probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession with intent to deliver drug paraphernalia in violation of 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(33) and sought review. The court affirmed. finding that the jury charge on statements made by buyers concerning their intended use of items purchased from appellant's store was proper; the statements were an aid in determining whether the products had a commonly recognized drug related function. The court found that the circumstantial evidence supporting appellant's conviction was sufficient. The search warrant was supported by an affidavit of an experienced narcotics agent who entered appellant's store and purchased a mask that was displayed with other items commonly used to aid in the ingestion of illegal drugs. The affidavit provided the magistrate with a rational, common sense basis for finding probable cause. The court found appellant's argument that he was prejudiced by the trial court, cutting short a line of questioning, was wholly without merit. Appellant was able to state to the jury, before questioning was cut short, that several officers had been in his store on prior occasions and had not mentioned the illegal nature of appellant's activity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed finding that the jury charge was proper, the circumstantial evidence was sufficient to support appellant's conviction, and the affidavit submitted in support of the search warrant provided the magistrate with sufficient facts to find probable cause. Appellant's claim that he was prejudiced by the trial court cutting off a line of questioning was meritless.

Hatchard v. Westinghouse Broadcasting Co.


Nos. 02219 Philadelphia 1982; 01056 Philadelphia 1985


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


350 Pa. Super. 1; 504 A.2d 211; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9276


August 6, 1985, Argued  
January 24, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted 7/29/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia county, Civil No. 5727 October Term 1979.

Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Civil No. 2409-C 1984.



DISPOSITION: On both appeals, the orders are reversed and the cases are remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, appellants sought review of orders from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia and Luzerne Counties (Pennsylvania), which granted appellees' motions to compel the production of certain documents that appellants sought to hold privileged under the Pennsylvania Shield Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5942(a) (1976).


OVERVIEW: In two consolidated appeals, the court addressed the issue of the production of reporter's notes, documentary material, as well as filmed "outtakes" prepared in connection with news broadcasts that appellees claimed were defamatory. The respective trial courts granted appellees' motions to compel such documents, and on review, appellants argued that these orders were erroneous because all the material was privileged under the Pennsylvania Shield Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5942(a) (1976). The court found that it was bound by precedent to hold that the Pennsylvania Shield Law absolutely protected from civil discovery all documentary material, notes, memorandum, films, and tapes, prepared in connection with news broadcasts. Accordingly, the orders were reversed and the cases were remanded.


OUTCOME: On both appeals, the orders compelling production of documents were reversed and the cases were remanded because the Pennsylvania Shield Law absolutely protected from civil discovery all documentary material prepared in connection with the news broadcasts that appellees claimed were defamatory.

Commonwealth v. Mourar


No. 3076 Philadelphia, 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


349 Pa. Super. 583; 504 A.2d 197; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9208


December 13, 1984, Argued  
January 21, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Chester County, No. 2468/2471-82.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence vacated and remanded for re-sentencing in accordance with this opinion. jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), for four criminal violations of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-101 et seq. Appellant alleged that the trial court erred in refusing to permit a withdrawal of his guilty plea and in applying sentencing guidelines.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered a guilty plea to four criminal violations of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-101 et seq. Three of the four offenses occurred after the effective date of the Sentencing Guidelines, 204 Pa. Code § 303 et seq. Because the prosecution was appellant's first contact with the criminal justice system, and he was offered no opportunity to benefit from penal discipline, the court held that appellant was not a habitual offender and that the trial court erred in enhancing the sentence for three of the offenses. The court held that the government was legally a "victim" entitled to restitution, but that the trial court erred in ordering restitution because the record did not reflect appellant's ability to make restitution. Because the court reversed and remanded for sentencing, the court did not address appellant's allegation that the court erred in refusing to permit a withdrawal of his guilty plea or that the sentence was excessive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and remanded for resentencing. Because the present convictions were appellant's first contact with the criminal justice system, the court held that appellant was not a habitual offender and the trial court erred in enhancing the sentence for three of the offenses. The court held that the trial court erred in ordering restitution because the court failed to determine appellant's ability to make restitution.

Commonwealth v. Cofoni


No. 935 Philadelphia, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


349 Pa. Super. 407; 503 A.2d 431; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9658


March 12, 1985, Argued  
January 10, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of March 7, 1984 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, No. 3437-83



DISPOSITION: Therefore, for the above reasons, judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him upon a plea of guilty of robbery, recklessly endangering another person, terroristic threats, possessing an instrument of crime and related charges in a case where defendant claimed the mandatory minimum sentencing provisions of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery, recklessly endangering another person, terroristic threats, possessing an instrument of crime, and related charges; he was sentenced under the mandatory minimum sentencing provisions of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712 (Act). Defendant sought review on the grounds that the Act was unconstitutional. On appeal the court rejected each claim of unconstitutionality and affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court held that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court had ruled that the Act did not alter the degree of guilt for the applicable crimes, that the required pre-trial notice of the crime for which a defendant was being prosecuted did not mandate notice that the Act would be applicable to a defendant at sentencing, and that the preponderance of the evidence standard used in a proceeding under the Act satisfied the requirements of due process. The court held the Act did not violate the separation of powers by conferring unlimited discretion upon the prosecutor because application of the Act was mandatory, not discretionary. The court also held the evidence presented by defendant established that his pistol was a firearm within the meaning of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for robbery, recklessly endangering another person, terroristic threats, and possessing an instrument of crime and the imposition of sentence under the mandatory minimum sentencing statute because the statute did not unconstitutionally violate due process or the separation of powers and defendant possessed a firearm within the statutory definition.

Commonwealth v. Sargent


No. 2951 Philadelphia, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


349 Pa. Super. 289; 503 A.2d 3; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9139


September 12, 1985, Argued  
January 3, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal at No. 8312-1743 & 1745


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of robbery and criminal conspiracy and imposed sentence under the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act, 42. Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9713 (Act), in a case where defendant claimed the prosecutor's argument was erroneous and that the Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and criminal conspiracy and sentence was imposed under the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9713 (Act). Defendant sought review and on appeal the court affirmed. The court held the prosecutor had reasonable latitude in arguing its case and permissibly argued that the evidence established defendant's guilt. The court rejected defendant's claims that the Act was unconstitutional. The court held the provisions of the Act concerning sentencing did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers of Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c) by controlling the practice and procedure in the courts. The court held that defendant failed to overcome the presumption of a statute's constitutionality because he did not establish that the burden of proof in sentencing was procedural and thus beyond the legislature's authority; there was authority characterizing this burden of proof as substantive. The court also held that defendant did not have standing to raise the issue that the Act was unconstitutional because it did not require notice prior to trial that it would be invoked because defendant received such notice prior to trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence defendant for robbery and criminal conspiracy because the prosecutor was permitted to argue that the evidence established defendant's guilt, and the mandatory minimum sentencing procedures did not violate the separation of powers by controlling the practice and procedure of the courts; it was not established that the statutory provisions were procedural and not substantive.

Boettger v. Loverro


Nos. 00984 PHILA. 1984, 1035 PHILA. 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


349 Pa. Super. 134; 502 A.2d 1310; 1986 Pa. Super. LEXIS 13588; 12 Media L. Rep. 1573


March 13, 1985, Argued  
January 3, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment in the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County, Civil No. 1982-C-2846



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and judgment entered for appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newspaper sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which awarded damages to appellee citizen under the Pennsylvania Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5725.


OVERVIEW: A transcript of appellee citizen's wiretapped conversations was inadvertently attached to an answer filed by the commonwealth in its criminal prosecution of appellee. The case file was not impounded and appellant newspaper published an article containing quotations from the transcript, which had been accessed by appellant's reporter. Appellee brought an action for unlawful disclosure or use under the Pennsylvania Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5725, and was awarded damages. On appeal, the court reversed and entered judgment for appellant because appellant was exempted from liability for publishing information of legitimate public concern that it had obtained from court records accessible to the public. Although the information in the transcript should have been confidential, appellant obtained it in lawful manner and reported it accurately. Section 5725 was enacted after the United States Supreme Court had upheld a similar constitutional right of publication, and because the legislature did not specifically exempt such publication, an exemption could be implied.


OUTCOME: Damage judgment against appellant newspaper for violations of the Pennsylvania Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act was reversed and judgment was entered for appellant. The legislature implied a statutory exemption that excepted appellant from liability for its publication of information of legitimate public concern regarding appellee citizen that appellant had lawfully obtained.

Commonwealth v. Murray


No. 02672 Philadelphia, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


348 Pa. Super. 439; 502 A.2d 624; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10407; 12 Media L. Rep. 1545


June 4, 1985, Argued  
December 20, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 83-08-3322, 3325, 3328.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), where he was convicted of aggravated assault and simple assault. Appellant asserted alleged hearsay, error in the admission of his religion and a prejudicial newspaper article, and that he had a right to a public trial under the Pennsylvania and Federal Constitutions, which right attached during a preliminary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate was accused of assaulting prison guards during an altercation. The preliminary hearing was held at the prison, rather than at the courthouse because of "security problems" related to the transportation of appellant. Appellant was convicted of aggravated assault and simple assault. Appellant sought review and asserted alleged hearsay, error in the admission of his religion and a prejudicial newspaper article, and that he had a right to a public trial under the Pennsylvania and Federal Constitutions and that this right attached during a preliminary hearing. On review, the court examined both the state and federal constitutions, paying specific attention to the right of an accused to have a public trial. The court noted that the lower court gave a cryptic explanation at the preliminary hearing regarding the decision to deny appellant a "public forum." The court concluded that there was presumed harm from an improperly closed preliminary hearing. However, in this case, the harm was cured during the public trial. The court therefore found that a remedy was not required, though appellant's constitutional rights were violated. The judgment was affirmed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for aggravated assault and simple assault because, although there was presumed harm from an improperly closed preliminary hearing, the court determined that the harm was cured during the public trial. The court reasoned that a remedy was not required even though appellant's constitutional rights were violated.

 

435 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Harvey


No. 744 Pittsburgh, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


348 Pa. Super. 544; 502 A.2d 679; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10436


April 9, 1985, Submitted  
December 20, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 24, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Westmoreland County, No. 169 of 1983.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of possession of a controlled substance and possession with intent to deliver. Appellant contended that his privacy rights and freedom of speech rights, under the constitution, were violated.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's conversations with an informant were recorded pursuant to the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5704. The informant consented to the recordings under the authorization in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5704(2)(ii). Appellant was convicted of possession of a controlled substance and possession with intent to deliver. The court affirmed. The wiretapping statute did not violate U.S. Const. amend. I or Pa. Const. art. I, §7. The statute did not have a chilling effect on free speech because it simply provided a means for law enforcement officials to preserve already admissible evidence of criminal activity for use in subsequent prosecutions. The informant could have testified about the conversations in the absence of the recordings. That the statute might lead criminals to engage in self-censorship did not render the statute unconstitutional. The statute also did not violate U.S. Const. amend. IV or Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. An individual's privacy interests were not violated if the other party to the conversations voluntarily consented to their recording; an individual assumed the risk that the party in whom he confided might misplace his trust.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for possession of a controlled substance and possession with intent to deliver. The wiretapping statute that authorized the recording of appellant's conversations with an informant did not violate the First or Fourth Amendments of the U.S. Constitution, or state law.

Commonwealth v. Kirchner


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


348 Pa. Super. 190; 501 A.2d 1134; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10535


September 23, 1985, Submitted  
December 6, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 4, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1435 Pittsburgh, 1984, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Nos. CC 8206439A and CC8206689A.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, convicted of robbery, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to a term of imprisonment for not less than five years nor more than 10 years pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712. Appellant argued that § 9712 was unconstitutional because, among other things, it allegedly violated double jeopardy principles.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of robbery. The court below sentenced appellant to a term of imprisonment for not less than five years nor more than 10 years pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712. Appellant argued on appeal that § 9712, which provided that any person convicted of certain enumerated offenses shall be sentenced to a minimum term of imprisonment of five years if he or she visibly possessed a firearm during the commission of the offense, was unconstitutional. The judgment of sentence was affirmed on appeal. First, the court rejected appellant's contention that § 9712 violated double jeopardy principles because § 9712 did not create a separate crime; it merely provided a minimum sentence to be imposed after appellant had been convicted. Moreover, contrary to appellant's contention, § 9712 in no way prevented a defendant from exercising his Pa. R. Crim. P. 1405(a) allocution rights. Finally, § 9712 was not invalid because it established only a mandatory minimum sentence because, when read together with Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1103, both minimum and maximum sentences for robbery committed with a firearm had been established.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which sentenced him to a term of imprisonment for not less than five years nor more than 10 years pursuant to a Pennsylvania law which mandated minimum sentences for certain enumerated offenses committed with a visible firearm, was affirmed. The sentencing statute did not violate double jeopardy principles, as contended by appellant, because it did not create a separate crime.

Commonwealth v. Taylor

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


348 Pa. Super. 256; 502 A.2d 195; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10188

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) Criminal Division, which dismissed his petition for relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA). Commonwealth v. Pelkey


No. 1042 Pittsburgh 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


349 Pa. Super. 373; 503 A.2d 414; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10189


September 11, 1985, Submitted  
November 29, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 6, 1986; Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied 10/16/86


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered August 13, 1984, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal No. CC8303704.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth challenged the decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) suppressing the results of a blood alcohol test performed on appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, while driving under the influence, struck another auto and injured one person, killing the other person in the car. When arriving at the scene of the crime the investigating officer noticed a strong odor of alcohol about appellee, and noticed that one person was killed while the other was seriously injured. Appellee was semiconscious. The officer requested that paramedics administer a blood alcohol test. The results were suppressed and appellant commonwealth challenged the ruling arguing that the test was valid under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(a)(1). The court agreed. Noting the semiconscious state of appellee, and the fact that there were serious injuries and death involved in the accident, the court found that implied consent was given under § 1547(a). The court found that probable cause or reasonable grounds warranted the officer's belief that a crime had been committed by appellee. The court therefore reversed the order suppressing the evidence.


OUTCOME: The order suppressing the blood alcohol test results from the test administered to appellee while semiconscious from an accident he had cause was reversed, because the investigating officer had reasonable grounds to believe that a crime had been committed by appellee in that a strong odor of alcohol was found on appellee and there were serious injuries and a death involved in the accident.

Commonwealth v. Kuphal


Nos. 02802, 02803, 02804 and Philadelphia 1983

DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant entered guilty pleas to three separate informations that charged him with robbery, theft, receiving stolen property, assault, terroristic threats, criminal mischief, and driving under the influence in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), and sought review of his sentence and contended that the sentencing guidelines, 24 Pa. Code § 303.1 et seq., were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered guilty pleas on a number of charges and sought review of his sentence, contended that the sentencing guidelines were unconstitutional. Appellant argued that the statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2151-2155, 9721, that created the commission on sentencing and the adoption of sentencing guidelines was unconstitutional; therefore, the guidelines were unconstitutional. Rudolph Rosa, Inc. v. Latrobe Brewing Co.


No. 1442 Pittsburgh, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


347 Pa. Super. 551; 500 A.2d 1194; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10092


April 11, 1985, Argued  
November 15, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 2, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Westmoreland County, No. 5460 of 1984.



DISPOSITION: The order entering judgment for appellee distributors is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant brewer sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania) that was in favor of appellee distributors in an action to determine whether appellant's termination of a distributor contract with appellees was in violation of the Liquor Code, 47 P.S. § 4-431.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brewer granted distributing rights to appellee distributors. The distributing rights agreements named appellant as primary distributor and provided for their cancellation upon notice of either party. Shortly after the agreements were entered into, the legislature amended the Liquor Code, 47 P.S. § 4-431, making it unlawful to terminate any distributing rights agreement without good cause shown. After appellant's employees went on strike, appellees refused to distribute for appellant beer that had been brewed out of state. Appellant terminated the distributing agreements, and suit was filed to determine whether this termination was unlawful under § 4-43l. The trial court ruled in favor of appellees, and appellant sought review. The court reversed. The court held that the "for cause" requirement by operation of 47 P.S. § 4-431(d)(5) did not apply to Pennsylvania manufacturers whose status as primary distributor existed prior to enactment of the amendment. The court further held that the newly enacted notice requirement did not have retroactive application. No statute was to be construed as retroactive unless clearly and manifestly intended by the legislature.


OUTCOME: Order entering judgment for appellee distributors was reversed. The statutory amendments requiring a showing of good cause of termination and providing for 90 days notice did not apply to appellant brewer because there was a specific exclusion for Pennsylvania manufacturers, and there was no evidence the legislature intended retroactive application.
