Commonwealth v. Irving


No. 00904 Philadelphia 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


347 Pa. Super. 349; 500 A.2d 868; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9973


March 12, 1985, Argued  
November 8, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal, at No. 3955-83.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of aggravated assault and sentenced him pursuant to the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act (Act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712. Appellant contended that the Act violated his right to equal protection of the law under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of two counts of aggravated assault and was sentenced to prison under the provisions of the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act (Act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the Act, contending it violated his right to equal protection of the law under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 2 because it applied to crimes committed with firearms but not those committed with other weapons. The court affirmed the sentence of appellant, finding that the Act did not violate his right to equal protection of the law because the sentence imposed under the Act, based upon proof of visible possession of a firearm, substantially served the legislative objective of warning potential criminals of the mandatory sentences they faced for conviction of the enumerated offenses. The court determined that the Act put potential criminals on notice of the minimum mandatory sentences for conviction, thus acting as a deterrent to violent crime and the use of firearms. The court concluded that appellant failed to overcome the presumption of constitutionality enjoyed by the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of appellant under the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act (Act), finding it did not violate his constitutional right to equal protection of the law, finding that sentences under the Act based upon proof of visible possession of a firearm fairly and substantially served the legislative objective of warning potential criminals of the mandatory sentences they faced for conviction of the enumerated offenses.

 

443 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Lawrence v. Lawrence


No. 02968 Philadelphia, 1984 AND 3020 PHILADELPHIA, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


347 Pa. Super. 57; 500 A.2d 154; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9765


June 21, 1985, Submitted  
October 25, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County, Civil Division, at No. 83-7763.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), denying her an award of alimony pendente lite, and appellee husband cross-appealed an award of counsel fees to appellant on the basis that appellee was the party who initiated the action thereby causing appellant to defend herself.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife sought review of an order from the trial court that awarded her no alimony pendente lite and appellee husband cross-appealed an order that directed him to pay counsel fees to appellant. The court affirmed the order that denied appellant alimony pendente lite and reversed the order that directed appellee to pay counsel fees to appellant. The court found that it was error for the trial court to award counsel fees to appellant for the reason that the divorce proceeding was initiated by appellee, thereby causing appellant to defend herself. The court determined that such a conclusion would imply that one who initiates a divorce proceeding could never recover counsel fees, no matter how needy that party might be. The court concluded that counsel fees could be awarded to either party in a divorce proceeding whenever it deemed such an award was necessary for either party to maintain or to defend a divorce action, and that the determination on which party pays should be made by examining the size of the moving party's estate, the value of counsel's services, and the ability to pay.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant wife an award of alimony pendente lite and reversed the order that awarded appellant counsel fees, finding that it was inappropriate for the trial court to order appellee husband to pay counsel fees to appellant for the reason that appellee initiated the divorce proceeding, thereby requiring appellant to defend herself.

Commonwealth v. Norris


No. 267 Harrisburg, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


346 Pa. Super. 351; 499 A.2d 644; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9609


March 12, 1985, Argued  
October 11, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County, Criminal Division, No. CC-45-83



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment of sentence following defendant's conviction for robbery. Defendant was sentenced to five to 15 years' imprisonment under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712(a), which required a mandatory five year sentence for crimes committed with a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and convicted of robbery after an officer noticed that defendant matched the description of one of the persons who had just committed a robbery a short distance away. Defendant was sentenced to five to 15 years' imprisonment under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712(a), which required a mandatory five year sentence for crimes committed with a firearm. Defendant argued that there was no probable cause for his arrest and that the application of § 9712(a) was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that defendant's equal protection rights were not violated because § 9712(a) was a reasonable means of achieving a legitimate legislative purpose and therefore satisfied the "rational basis" test. The court found that defendant's suspicious conduct, coupled with his matching the description of the robbery suspect, gave the officer probable cause to arrest him. The court concluded that, in enacting § 9712, the legislature was concerned with curbing violent crimes resulting from the use of firearms. Because defendant did not belong to a "suspect class," and no fundamental interest was involved, the "rational basis" standard applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence following defendant's robbery conviction, holding that defendant's equal protection rights were not violated because the mandatory sentencing provision was a reasonable means of achieving a legitimate legislative purpose and therefore satisfied the "rational basis" test. In addition, defendant's arrest was supported by probable cause.

 

446 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Taylor


No. 354 Pittsburgh, 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


346 Pa. Super. 599; 500 A.2d 110; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8859


December 13, 1984, Argued  
October 4, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted June 26, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of March 4, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division No. 1522 of 1982



DISPOSITION: For these reasons, we affirm the judgment of sentence of the court below and dismiss the appeal of the District Attorney of Erie County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth sought review of a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, (Pennsylvania), which did not impose a deadly weapon enhancement pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9721(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with aggravated assault. There was no designation as to whether the charge was based upon intent to cause serious bodily injury or upon intent to cause serious bodily injury with a deadly weapon. Defendant pled guilty to the charges. The factual basis for the plea was that defendant had caused great bodily harm to the victim. During the sentencing hearing, the victim testified that defendant threatened her and held a knife to her throat. The trial court refused to impose an enhanced sentence under the Commonwealth's claim that the knife was a deadly weapon because use of a weapon was not the factual basis for the guilty plea. The Commonwealth claimed on appeal that the trial court had discretion in imposing an enhanced sentence. The court rejected the Commonwealth's claim and affirmed the sentence. The court ruled that the due process clause applied to sentencing procedures and that defendant could not be sentenced upon a factual basis that was not admitted into evidence at the guilty plea. The court also ruled that the weapon enhancement provision in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9721 for possession of a deadly weapon was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence by the trial court. The court ruled that defendant could not be given an enhanced sentence for aggravated assault while in possession of a deadly weapon because the factual basis for defendant's guilty plea was based upon bodily harm, and that the sentence enhancement for possession of a deadly weapon was unconstitutionally vague.

Commonwealth v. Saul


No. 364 Harrisburg, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


346 Pa. Super. 155; 499 A.2d 358; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8679


June 21, 1985, Submitted  
September 27, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 29, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of July 18, 1984 in the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division, No. 2243 CD 1983.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which found defendant guilty of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance and sentenced him to serve three to twenty-three months incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised two issues on appeal concerning his conviction and sentence for unlawful delivery of a controlled substance. The first was whether the detective involved with the case had illegally conducted a police investigation outside his geographical jurisdiction without statutory authority. The second related issue was whether a motion to suppress was the proper method to attack an illegal investigation and its consequential production of evidence. The court held that the detective involved with the case clearly acted outside the scope of his statutory authority when he conducted an investigation outside his geographical jurisdiction. However, the court also held that, under the circumstances of the instant case, suppression of the evidence gained by the detective's illegal investigation was a remedy all out of proportion to the benefits gained to the end of obtaining justice while preserving individual liberties unimpaired. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court, which found defendant guilty of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance. The court held that the detective involved with the case clearly acted outside the scope of his authority when he conducted an investigation outside his jurisdiction. However, the court also held that suppression of the evidence gained by the detective was a disproportionate remedy.

Commonwealth v. Goodman


No. 00656 Pittsburgh 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


347 Pa. Super. 403; 500 A.2d 1117; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8806


July 23, 1984, Argued  
September 27, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied December 6, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal at No. CC8204435.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state Attorney General's Office challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which suppressed evidence in a criminal complaint charging appellee with theft of telephone services. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in holding that the scope of appellant's authority to prosecute was specifically limited by the Commonwealth Attorneys Act, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 732-205(a)(1)-(8) (1980).


OVERVIEW: Appellant state Attorney General's Office challenged a judgment holding that appellant lacked authority to prosecute appellee attorney under the Commonwealth Attorneys Act, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 732-205(a)(1)-(8) (1980). Appellee was charged with theft of phone services for using an electronic device to circumvent the phone company's billing apparatus. Prior to the preliminary hearing, appellant determined that a basis for appellant's prosecution did not exist, and turned over to the county prosecutor. The trial court suppressed the evidence on the grounds that prior to application for a search warrant appellant did not have a reasonable belief that it could prosecute appellee. Holding that scope of appellant's authority to investigate a criminal offense was limited by the statute to cases that appellant had the power to prosecute, the appellate court rejected appellant's contention that it reasonably believed it could prosecute under the state racketeering statute. However, the appellate court reversed the suppression order because appellee's constitutional rights were not violated, considering that there was probable cause to support the issuance of the search warrant.


OUTCOME: The suppression court's order was reversed because while appellant state Attorney General's Office exceeded its authority there was no showing that appellee's right to be free from unreasonable searches was violated. The court remanded the case because there was no argument that probable cause supported the issuance of the search warrant, or that the challenged search warrant was not issued by a neutral and disinterested judicial officer.

Commonwealth v. Cieri


No. 02280 PHILA. 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


346 Pa. Super. 77; 499 A.2d 317; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8664


March 27, 1985, Argued  
September 18, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal No. 3041-83.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of driving under the influence of alcohol or a controlled substance, involuntary manslaughter, reckless endangerment, and driving under the influence. The trial court also imposed a mandatory sentence under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3735.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in a vehicle accident resulting in death. Defendant was transported to the hospital where blood was withdrawn, which later tested positive for alcohol. Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol or a controlled substance, involuntary manslaughter, and related crimes. The appellate court affirmed. Defendant did have a privacy interest in the blood taken from him without a warrant. However, under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(a), defendant consented to the search because there was probable cause to believe that he was intoxicated. The consent was implied from the fact that there was a serious accident, defendant was semi-conscious, the police smelled alcohol, and marijuana was found in his vehicle. The trial court had the discretion to refuse to allow a witness to testify where he would only have exercised his privilege against self-incrimination. Defendant failed to allege in his motion to set aside the conviction why the verdict was against the weight of the evidence, and the issue was not preserved for review. The mandatory sentence of defendant under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3735 was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol or a controlled substance, involuntary manslaughter, and related crimes was affirmed. Defendant gave his implied consent to a blood test because under the vehicular consent statute, there was probable cause that he was intoxicated due to his semi-conscious state and the odor of alcohol. Defendant's mandatory sentence was constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Leta


Superior Court of Pennsylvania

Commonwealth v. Palmer


No. 1781 Philadelphia, 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


345 Pa. Super. 416; 498 A.2d 891; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8504


June 5, 1985, Argued  
September 6, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. 463-465 January 1977


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered a murder conviction. Defendant claimed a bring-down order authorizing his transport to police headquarters for questioning violated the habeas corpus statute, 12 P.S. § 1887. Defendant claimed a new trial was required because his confession was involuntary, and he was denied assistance of counsel during questioning.


OVERVIEW: An elderly woman was murdered. Police questioned defendant and released him for a lack of probable cause to arrest. Later, defendant was taken into custody for a rape. A "bring-down" order was entered which authorized police to transport defendant to police headquarters for an interrogation regarding the murder. During questioning, police told defendant he was being arrested for the murder. Defendant confessed. The confession was used at trial, and defendant was convicted. Defendant appealed. Defendant claimed that the "bring-down" order violated the habeas corpus statute, 12 P.S. § 1887, because it was not signed by a judge, but merely approved by the prosecutor's office and stamped by a court clerk. Defendant also claimed he was denied assistance of counsel during interrogation. The appellate court affirmed. Because there was probable cause to arrest, and defendant waived his right to counsel, suppression of the confession was denied. No new trial was required. Although defendant alleged no prejudice from the bring-down itself, the court found that the bring-down order was an invasion of defendant's rights. Thus, court orders were required for future bring-downs.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's entry of defendant's murder conviction because although a bring-down order to transport defendant for questioning was not signed by the court, there was probable cause to arrest defendant and suppression of a subsequent confession was unnecessary. No new trial was required because defendant waived his right to counsel at questioning.

Commonwealth v. Bannister


No. 01890 Philadelphia 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


345 Pa. Super. 178; 497 A.2d 1362; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8525


May 21, 1985, Submitted  
September 6, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE JUNE 8, 1984 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, CRIMINAL NO. 83-04-1408, 1409, 1410, 1412, 1413
Commonwealth v. Bannister, 11 Phila. 238, 1984 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 81 (Pa. C.P., 1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) pursuant to the firearms provision of the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and various other crimes when he used a firearm and shot a victim in the leg to force the surrender of the victim's wallet. Defendant was sentenced to 5 to 10 years incarceration in accordance with the firearms provision of the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712. Section 9712 required a minimum sentence of five years' confinement for visible possession of a firearm during the commission of various offenses, including robbery. Defendant appealed the judgment, challenging the constitutionality of the statute. The court affirmed the judgment and upheld the statute pursuant to a recent decision of the state's high court, which addressed the same issues that defendant raised. The court provided that requiring only a preponderance of the evidence for visible possession of the firearm satisfied the minimum requirements of due process. Additionally, the power to determine the punishment for criminal conduct was within the province of the legislature, and the trial court was not deprived of its power to sentence under the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence of 5 to 10 years' confinement of the trial court because the statute addressing mandatory sentencing where a firearm was visible during the commission of certain crimes was constitutional under a state high court decision. The statute satisfied the minimum requirements of due process and did not violate the separation of powers doctrine by depriving the trial court of its discretion in sentencing.

Grassmyer v. Coleman


No. 532 Harrisburg, 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


345 Pa. Super. 358; 498 A.2d 441; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8542


May 1, 1985, Argued  
September 6, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Civil Division, No. 83-20, 895



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother appealed an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County (Pennsylvania), affirming a family court hearing officer's order dismissing a paternity suit as time-barred under the statute of limitations, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(e). Plaintiff alleged defendant putative father's acknowledgement of paternity operated as a waiver of the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Defendant putative father allegedly executed an acknowledgment of paternity for a child that was born out of wedlock Almost eight years later, plaintiff mother brought an action for support. A family court hearing officer found the suit was time-barred under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(e) because the action was not brought within six years of the child's birth, or two years of the acknowledgement. The trial court affirmed the order. Plaintiff appealed, claiming the acknowledgement acted as a waiver of the statute of limitations, and that defendant was estopped from raising the statute of limitations because he acknowledged paternity. The appellate court affirmed. The court found it was inequitable to suddenly find a duty to support the acknowledgment was more than seven years before the filing of an action. This acknowledgment was not a reasonable basis for instituting a paternity proceeding. An acknowledgement within the six-year period did not fix the right to pursue an action, but only fixed the time frame during which such action could be pursued should it carry beyond the basic six-year limitation. Because plaintiff failed to timely institute suit, her suit was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order affirming a family court hearing officer's dismissal of plaintiff mother's paternity suit against defendant putative father. Because plaintiff did not sue within two years of an alleged acknowledgment of paternity, or bring a paternity action within six years of the child's birth, the action was time-barred under the statute of limitations, and to allow such a suit would be unfair to defendant.

Faust v. Messinger


No. 00057


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


345 Pa. Super. 155; 497 A.2d 1351; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8391


May 23, 1985, Argued  
August 23, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted 3/31/86; (510 Pa. 246; 507 A.2d 369).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of December 6, 1984, in the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County, Civil Division, at Nos. 1984-C-1616 and 1982-1149.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grandmother sought review from an order in the Civil Division of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which denied her visitation rights with her grandson following his adoption.


OVERVIEW: Appellant grandmother's son-in-law killed appellant's daughter. He was convicted and sentenced to prison. Appellant's grandson was subsequently adopted by appellee adoptive parents in a proceeding where appellant's son-in-law relinquished his parental rights. Approximately a year and a half later, appellant learned of her grandson's whereabouts and the fact of his adoption. She filed both a complaint for custody and a petition to intervene in the adoption nunc pro tunc. At the close of the pleadings, the matters were consolidated and upon motion for judgment on the pleadings, the trial court dismissed her claim for custody and her request to intervene in the adoption proceeding. The court affirmed the trial court order dismissing appellant's complaint for custody and to intervene in the adoption proceeding. The court noted that the Custody and Grandparents Visitation Act, 23 P.S. § 1001 et seq. did not provide appellant with the requirement of notice that was protected by the due process clause. The court also noted that, for sound public policy reasons, this same act terminated visitation once a child was adopted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing appellant grandmother's claim for custody and her petition to intervene in the adoption proceeding, and held that appellant was not entitled to notice of the adoption proceeding, and, since the adoption was valid, appellant's statutory entitlement to seek visitation with her grandson ended when the adoption decree was granted.

Commonwealth v. Stock


No. 01334 Pittsburgh 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


346 Pa. Super. 60; 499 A.2d 308; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7884


April 11, 1985, Argued  
August 16, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 16, 1985. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 28, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE NOVEMBER 4, 1983 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF ALLEGHENY COUNTY, CRIMINAL NO. CC8108442



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.

Remand for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a ruling of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of selling obscene magazines in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903(a)(2) and ordered defendant to pay $ 10,000 rather than serve his prison term.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of selling obscene magazines in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903(a)(2) and ordered to pay $ 10,000 rather than serve his prison term. Defendant challenged his conviction and sentence on multiple grounds and claimed that § 5903(a)(2) was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's conviction and sentence. The court found that § 5903(a)(2) was not overly broad such that a chilling effect was created where, although the statute on its face prohibited any showing of obscene materials, the legislative intent was properly interpreted as prohibiting only the public showing of such materials or the showing of such materials for commercial gain. The court found that § 5903(a)(2) did not violate the constitutional rights to privacy, as it was not substantially overbroad in relation to its purpose of regulating the commercial sale of obscenity, activity not protected by U.S.Const.amend. I. The court affirmed the $ 10,000 fine accessed as within the trial court's discretion where based on defendant's ability to pay even where the fine would cause defendant substantial hardship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which convicted defendant of selling obscene magazines and ordered defendant to pay $ 10,000 rather than serve his prison term. The court found that the state obscenity statute was constitutional and that the fine imposed, while likely to cause defendant significant hardship, was within the trial court's discretion and based on defendant's ability to pay.

Stat. § 5701 et seq., was unconstitutional or, if valid, was improperly applied in this case. The court deemed the Pennsylvania Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act (the Act) constitutional and held that the Act was properly applied in this case to obtain the phone tap which produced most of the commonwealth's evidence.

Commonwealth v. Barnhart

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


345 Pa. Super. 10; 497 A.2d 616; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8268


March 5, 1985, Argued  
August 16, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 14, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence June 29, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County, Criminal No. 0424 and 0425, 1982.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence as modified affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged a ruling of the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendants of involuntary manslaughter and endangering the welfare of a child.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of involuntary manslaughter in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2504 and endangering the welfare of a child in violation of Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4304 after defendants' 2 year-old child died from an untreated tumor when defendants' religious beliefs caused them to not seek medical attention to prevent the child's death. Defendants challenged the convictions on multiple grounds including a claim that the state criminal laws could not constitutionally punish conduct protected by U.S.Const. amend. I. The court overruled this claim and affirmed the involuntary manslaughter conviction. The court vacated the endangerment conviction and held that defendants could not be convicted of both crimes under double jeopardy provisions because a sole criminal act was at issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and vacated in part the trial court ruling, which convicted defendants of involuntary manslaughter and endangering the welfare of a child. The court affirmed the involuntary manslaughter conviction and held that punishment for failure to seek medical treatment did not violate defendants' freedom of religion rights. The court vacated the endangerment conviction on double jeopardy grounds.

Commonwealth v. Molina


No. 00843 Philadelphia, 1983


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


344 Pa. Super. 459; 496 A.2d 1196; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7771


May 22, 1985, Submitted  
August 2, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of March 16, 1983, in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 82-08-155.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prosecution sought review of the judgment of sentence entered in the Criminal Division of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania). Appellant contended that the trial court's determination that the Mandatory Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712, was unconstitutional was erroneous.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted of possessing an instrument of crime in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 907, and aggravated assault, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2702(a)(1). After the jury returned its verdict and before sentencing, appellant prosecution notified appellee of its intent to invoke 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. An. § 9712. Following the denial of appellee's post-trial motions, the trial court refused to apply the mandatory minimum sentencing statute and imposed a sentence of probation for both convictions. Appellant filed a motion for reconsideration of the sentence, which the trial court denied. The court reversed the trial court's decision. The court noted that, although the trial court purported to find the statute unconstitutional, it failed to attach any cases that supported a constitutional underpinning of its decision. Rather, it was clear that the trial court simply believed that appellant's situation warranted an exercise of discretion for imposition of a lesser sentence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence imposed for aggravated assault and remanded the case for resentencing and relinquished jurisdiction. The court found that the mandatory sentencing statute did not violate due process and the trial court did not have the authority to ignore it.

Commonwealth v. Sterling


No. 00908 Pittsburgh 1984


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


344 Pa. Super. 269; 496 A.2d 789; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8193


March 26, 1985, Argued  
July 26, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied 1/21/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, at No. 210 of 1984.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant robber, convicted of robbery, corruption of minors, and criminal conspiracy, sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which ruled that a carbon dioxide powered pellet gun was a "firearm" under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712(e) and imposed the mandatory minimum sentence of imprisonment for the use of a firearm during the commission of a felony.


OVERVIEW: Appellant robber pled guilty to robbery, corruption of minors and criminal conspiracy. The trial court determined that a carbon dioxide powered pellet gun was a "firearm" under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712(e), and thus the mandatory minimum sentence of imprisonment for the use of a firearm during commission of a robbery was applicable. The statute defined a firearm as a weapon, including a starter gun, which was or designed or could be converted to expel a projectile by an explosive or the expansion of gas therein. Appellant sought review, arguing that the trial court erred by ruling that the pellet gun was a firearm under § 9712(e). Appellant maintained that the definition of "weapon" found in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 907 of the Crimes Code governed. The court affirmed, holding that the language of § 9712(e) was clear and unambiguous and withstood appellant's vagueness attack, the pellet gun expelled a projectile by action of an explosive or expansion of gas, and that the introductory phrase in § 9712(e) "as used in this section 'firearm means'" evinced the legislature's awareness of other definitions of firearm in other sections.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant robber's sentence for robbery, corruption of minors, and criminal conspiracy. The court held that a carbon dioxide powered pellet gun was a firearm under the Sentencing Code.

 

463 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Williams


No. 663 October 1978


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


344 Pa. Super. 108; 496 A.2d 31; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7562


February 3, 1984, Submitted  
July 19, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENTS OF SENTENCE OF THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS, CRIMINAL DIVISION PHILADELPHIA COUNTY AT NOS. 1187, 1190 MAY TERM 1977
Commonwealth v. Williams, 277 Pa. Super. 86, 419 A.2d 673, 1980 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2295 (1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought reversal of the sentences imposed upon him by the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) for his convictions for aggravated assault, possession of a prohibited offensive weapon, carrying a firearm on a public street, and resisting arrest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought reversal of his sentences for aggravated assault, possession of a prohibited offensive weapon, carrying a firearm on a public street, and resisting arrest. He alleged that he was unlawfully and duplicitously sentenced to consecutive maximum terms of imprisonment, as all of these convictions resulted from the single unlawful act of firing a sawed-off shotgun, and, that they merged for sentencing purposes. The court vacated the sentence for possessing a prohibited offensive weapon, but affirmed the remaining sentences. The court held that the charges for aggravated assault and resisting arrest did not merge with each other, nor did they merge with carrying a firearm. The court found that carrying a firearm did not merge with aggravated assault, as a conviction under the firearm statute did not merge with a violent offense, and no valid reason existed for merging the firearm violation with resisting arrest. However, the firearm offense and the prohibited offensive weapons charge merged. Possession of the same gun in the same circumstances was proof of crimes that involved each other and the statutes protected two diverse interests of the Commonwealth.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence for possessing a prohibited offensive weapon because that offense merged with the carrying a firearm offense. The court affirmed the remaining sentences because the charges for aggravated assault and resisting arrest did not merge with each other, and they did not merge with carrying a firearm.

 

464 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Cummins v. Firestone Tire & Rubber Co.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


344 Pa. Super. 9; 495 A.2d 963; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9561; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P10,616


March 4, 1985, Argued  
July 5, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied 10/16/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of January 25, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division, at No. GD 82-08884.



DISPOSITION: For the preceding reasons, we affirm the order of the lower court sustaining appellees' preliminary objections and remand this case for the disposition of all remaining preliminary motions in a manner consistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction is not retained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania) dismissing his complaint for injuries resulting from a defective tire and rim assembly. Appellant alleged causes of action for negligence, strict liability, industry-wide liability, concert of action, and market share liability.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sued appellees for damages for injuries he received when his tire and multi-piece rim assembly exploded. He alleged causes of action for negligence, strict liability, industry-wide liability, market share liability, and concerted action liability. The trial court sustained preliminary objections by appellees, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that appellant's claims based on negligence and strict liability failed. The court found that appellant failed to designate whether the tire, or the rim assembly was defective, or identify one of the appellees, as the manufacturer, supplier, or seller of the defective product. Because negligence and strict liability attached liability only to the entity that sold the defective product, he failed to establish the causal connection between appellees' conduct and his injuries. With regard to appellant's claims for industry-wide liability and concert of action, the court determined that those causes of action were not recognized in Pennsylvania, and refused to adopt them in this case. The court found that appellant failed to allege facts supporting market share liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment as appellant failed to designate whether the tire or the rim assembly was defective and failed to identify the manufacturer, supplier, or seller part. Appellant's theories of industry-wide liability and concert of action were not recognized in Pennsylvania and facts were not alleged supporting market share liability.

Symbula v. Johns-Manville Corp.


No. 2531 Philadelphia, 1982


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


343 Pa. Super. 541; 495 A.2d 598; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7615; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P10,605


March 27, 1985, Submitted  
June 28, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, No. 4052 (131) Case No. 69 Jan. T. 1979



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executrix, in her wrongful death and survival actions, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment to appellee corporations, holding that appellant's claims were barred by the statutes of limitations and that the discovery rule did not apply.


OVERVIEW: Appellant executrix brought wrongful death and survival actions against appellee corporations, alleging her husband died from exposure to asbestos products manufactured by appellee corporations. The lower court held that the wrongful death action was time-barred by the one-year statute of limitations, 12 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1603 (current version at 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5524(2)), the survival action was time-barred under the two-year statute of limitations, 12 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 34 (current version at § 5524(2)), and the discovery rule did not toll the limitations. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in the wrongful death action, but vacated the summary judgment in the survival action, holding that the discovery rule applied where an action was commenced after the effective date of § 5524(2) (June 27, 1978), and the date of death was on or after June 16, 1977 for a wrongful death action, or June 16, 1976 for a survival action; and because the decedent died on September 27, 1976 and the action was bought on January 24, 1979, the wrongful death action was barred by the prior statutes of limitation, but the discovery rule was applicable to the survival action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment for appellee corporations in appellant executrix's wrongful death action, because appellant did not bring her cause of action within the then-existing statute of limitations and thus the discovery rule did not apply; but appellant did do so with respect to her survival action, and the court vacated summary judgment and remanded for reinstatement of the complaint.

Solvent Machinery & Filter Systems, Inc. v. Teamsters Local No. 115


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


343 Pa. Super. 505; 495 A.2d 579; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8165


January 17, 1985, Submitted  
June 28, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] An Appeal from the Order of the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas, Docket No. 82-7633-06-5. Civil Division.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a labor union, members, and union officers, sought review of an order of the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which issued a preliminary injunction against appellants picketing appellee employer's business.


OVERVIEW: Appellee employer sold and closed its plant, resulting in the loss of jobs. Appellants, a labor union, members, and union officers, picketed appellee's plant. Appellee obtained a preliminary injunction against appellant. The court reversed the order granting the preliminary injunction, holding that the trial court erred in its finding that the Labor Anti-Injunction Act, 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 206d(d). The court held that instead Pa 43 Cons. Stat. Ann. § 206I governed the facts and allowed injunctions only in limited circumstances of which no evidence existed. The court therefore reversed the order and remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded an order granting a preliminary injunction to appellee employer to preclude appellant, a labor union, members, and union officers, from picketing appellee's business because a statutory labor anti-injunction act applied to the facts, because no damage or seizure to appellee's property was found.

 

467 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hoover


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


343 Pa. Super. 372; 494 A.2d 1131; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9375


March 11, 1985, Argued  
June 14, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied 8/18/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the judgment of sentence of July 2, 1984 in the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Criminal Division, at No. 1983-652.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of sentence by the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of drunk driving and sentenced him to a 90 day prison term and a $ 1000 fine under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1543(b). Appellant argued that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated the equal protection clause, imposed cruel and unusual punishment, and violated the separation of powers doctrine.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol on a suspended license. Pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1543(b), he was sentenced to 90 days in prison and a $ 1000 fine. Appellant argued that the statute was unconstitutional and that it had been applied to him retroactively. The court held that the statute was constitutional for the same reasons that a companion statute on drunk driving had survived a constitutional challenge. Enactment of statutes that defined criminal conduct and imposed penalties for it was a proper exercise of legislative power and did not violate the separation of powers doctrine. The statute did not impose cruel and unusual punishment because its penalties met current societal standards of decency. On the equal protection challenge, the statute met the rational basis test because it was rationally related to the commonwealth's legitimate interest in highway safety. Like the recidivism statutes, these penalties did not impose additional punishments for a drunk driving conviction. Nor did the fine violate due process when appellant could pay on installments and could not be imprisoned for failure to pay it. The conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for driving under the influence of alcohol. The statute under which appellant was charged was constitutional. The statute was rationally related to a legitimate state interest in highway safety. Neither the prison sentence nor the fine contradicted contemporary standards of justice. The enactment of statutes that define criminal conduct was a proper exercise of legislative power.

Moore v. Smith


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


343 Pa. Super. 326; 494 A.2d 1107; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7374


March 6, 1985, Argued  
May 17, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied July 12, 1985. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 16, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 00163 Phila., 1984, Appeal from the Order Entered on December 22, 1983 in the court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 1939 June Term, 1982.
Moore v. Smith, 10 Phila. 301, 1984 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 26 (Pa. C.P., 1984)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former owner sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered a summary judgement against her in a tax lien action on her property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former owner sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered a summary judgment against her in a tax lien action on her property. Appellant was not served notice of the tax lien action nor was she given notice of the assessment or lien or of the writ of execution. Appellant continued to live at the property, the property was sold at a sheriff's sale, and appellee new owner filed a complaint in ejectment against appellant. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee, holding that the Municipal Claims and Tax Liens Act, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7102, provided that real estate taxes imposed by the city were a first lien against the property. The court found that the act had no provision for notifying a property owner prior to filing a tax lien but only that the sheriff post the property and follow the statutory requirements regarding service and publication of the sale. The court found that these statutory requirements were met, therefore finding that the order against appellant be affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee new owner in a tax lien action where appellant former owner's property was sold without notice to her because no statutory requirement for notice existed and all requirements for a tax sale were properly fulfilled.

Commonwealth v. Maldonado


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


343 Pa. Super. 154; 494 A.2d 402; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7842


May 22, 1984, Argued  
May 3, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 28, 1985. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 10, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal, at No. 1722, 1723, 1724, 1726 October Term, 1980. No 00206 Philadelphia 1983

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal, at No. 1727, 1728, 1729, and 1731 October Term, 1980. No. 00492 Philadelphia 1983


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, older and younger brother, challenged their convictions by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), for second degree murder, criminal conspiracy, robbery, and the possession of instruments of crime. Appellants alleged that the evidence was insufficient to support their convictions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, older and younger brother, armed themselves with knives and stole a box that belonged to one of a group of young people socializing in a park. Two young men pursued appellants. During the pursuit, the box was discarded. One of the young men was later found unconscious; he had been stabbed twice in the back and later died. Appellants escaped. Appellant older brother admitted that he stabbed the decedent. Appellants were convicted of second degree murder, criminal conspiracy, robbery, and the possession of instruments of crime. Appellants alleged that the evidence was insufficient to support their convictions. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions. The court held that the evidence was more than sufficient to sustain appellants' convictions for criminal conspiracy and possession of instruments of crime. Further, the court found that there was fresh pursuit of appellants and no break in the chain of events from the time the box was stolen until the decedent was murdered. The decedent was killed during the perpetration of the robbery. Therefore, the court ruled that the evidence was sufficient to support a conviction for the crime of murder in the second degree.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for appellants, older and younger brother, for criminal conspiracy and possession of instruments of crime because the evidence was more than sufficient to sustain those verdicts. Also, the evidence was sufficient to support a conviction for the crime of murder in the second degree because there was a robbery and the decedent was killed during the perpetration of that robbery.

Patton v. Republic Steel Corp.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


342 Pa. Super. 101; 492 A.2d 411; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7246


September 27, 1983, Argued  
April 26, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 442 PITTSBURGH, 1982, Appeal from the Order of April 22, 1982, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division, at No. GD 81-27859.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee corporation's preliminary objections and dismissed appellants' complaint that the subsidence damage to their home had been caused by appellee's negligence. Appellants contended that they were statutorily entitled to proceed with their negligence claim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant homeowners brought an action against appellee corporation in which they claimed that the subsidence to their home had been caused by the negligence of appellee. Appellants claimed that appellee commenced or recommenced coal mining operations that caused appellants' land to shift and subside, resulting in damage to appellants' home. The trial court granted appellee's preliminary objections and dismissed appellants' complaint. The court reversed and remanded. The court held that the Bituminous Mine Subsidence and Land Conservation Act of 1966 (BMSLCA), 52 P.S. § 1406.1 et seq., imposed a duty on appellee to give notice to appellants of its intent to mine, which duty appellants' complaint claimed appellee failed to meet. It held that appellants' complaint thus stated a cause of action under BMSLCA. The court further held that appellants' complaint also stated a cause of action under the BMSLCA of July 2, 1937, 52 P.S. § 1407 because the complaint unambiguously averred that appellants' home suffered subsidence damage as a result of negligent mining activities by appellee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's dismissal on appellee corporation's preliminary objections of appellant homeowners' complaint that the subsidence damage to their home had been caused by appellee's negligence and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that appellants were statutorily entitled to proceed with their negligence claim.

Hoch v. Philadelphia Electric Co.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


341 Pa. Super. 598; 492 A.2d 27; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7152


October 9, 1984, Argued  
April 19, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 02252 PHL 83, Appeal from the Order entered July 26, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, at No. 82-9029.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condemnee challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee condemnor's demurrers and dismissed appellant's actions for trespass, conspiracy, nuisance, and unconstitutional taking arising from appellee's actions in clearing a right-of-way on appellant's land in order to construct a high voltage transmission line.


OVERVIEW: Appellee condemnor's employee entered appellant condemnee's land to clear a right-of-way granted by appellant's predecessor to construct and maintain a high voltage transmission line. Consequently, appellant's property was damaged and the transmission line caused electromagnetic radiation, noise, and vibrations. Appellant filed a suit for trespass, conspiracy, nuisance, and unconstitutional taking against appellee. The trial court sustained appellee's demurrers and dismissed appellant's claims. On appeal, the court reversed in part, affirmed in part, and held the unconstitutional taking claim was properly dismissed because appellant was not a property owner named against whom appellee's eminent domain power was to be exercised, hence, the procedures for determining damages did not come into play. As such, the proper remedy was a viewer's proceeding and appellant failed to petition for the appointment of viewers. Also, there was no challenge to the validity or scope of the condemnation. The court found a cause of action existed for trespass because appellee did not aver it was a successor of the grantee and only reasonable increases in the burden of the easement was permitted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the trespass, conspiracy, and nuisance claims, affirmed the dismissal of the unconstitutional taking claim, and held because appellee condemnor had no taxing power against appellant condemnee, the proper remedy was a viewer's proceeding but appellant failed to petition for an appointment of viewers. Also, there was a claim for trespass because appellee did not aver they were the successors of the grantee.

Commonwealth v. Checca


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


341 Pa. Super. 480; 491 A.2d 1358; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6980


November 20, 1984, Argued  
April 12, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 7, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 361 Pittsburgh, 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Criminal No. 1153 of 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, (Pennsylvania), which imposed a term of 11 and 1/2 to 24 months imprisonment and payment of the costs of prosecution, following appellant's conviction by a jury of official oppression.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for official oppression because the trial court did not err in refusing to suppress the tapes and evidence of electronic interceptions when they were properly authorized and the search warrant based thereon was properly made. The court found that appellant was not deprived of the opportunity to raise the entrapment defense because there was no evidence to support a finding that the woman was acting as an agent of law enforcement officials, and her prior status as an informant was irrelevant. The trial judge properly denied appellant's request for an instruction on the entrapment defense because no evidence was offered to show that the initial contacts between appellant and woman were instigated or encouraged by law enforcement. The court found that appellant admitted he initiated discussions regarding sexual favors, and he offered no evidence whatsoever to show that his conduct was so induced. The court concluded that consent was not an issue because appellant's action in using his official position to take advantage of a defendant whose case was pending before him was conduct covered by the official oppression statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for official oppression because the electronic interceptions that incriminated appellant were properly authorized, the search warrant based thereon was properly made, and appellant was not deprived of the opportunity to raise the entrapment defense because there was no evidence to support a finding that the woman was acting as an agent of law enforcement.

Commonwealth v. Cooke


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


342 Pa. Super. 58; 492 A.2d 63; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6849


October 10, 1984, Submitted  
April 4, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 16, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 02431 PHL 83, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of September 2, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Criminal Division, at No. 1266 of 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of attempted robbery and conspiracy and sentenced him to a term of imprisonment of 5-10 years on the attempted robbery charge, and to a concurrent term of 10 years probation on the conspiracy charge.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's sentence for attempted robbery, but vacated his concurrent sentence for criminal conspiracy because he was improperly convicted and sentenced for two inchoate crimes, criminal attempt and criminal conspiracy, which was prohibited under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 906. The court concluded that the pretrial publicity, which stressed the applicability of the new mandatory sentencing statute; quoted a police captain's statement that appellant would get "no deals;" noted that co-defendant had given police a statement implicating appellant; and reported the revocation of appellant's parole on a prior criminal charge, was not so sustained and inflammatory as to be inherently prejudicial and deny him a fair trial. The court found that the reporting was basically factual, the four articles could not constitute "pervasive dissemination" of the information, and three months had elapsed between the last of the four articles and the beginning of appellant's trial. The court concluded that a police officer's remark that he knew appellant from speaking to him before could not be construed by the jury that appellant had been involved in prior criminal conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's sentence for attempted robbery, but vacated his sentence for criminal conspiracy because it was improper for the trial court to convict and sentence appellant for two inchoate crimes, criminal attempt and criminal conspiracy, under state law.

Eichenlaub v. Eichenlaub


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


340 Pa. Super. 552; 490 A.2d 918; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6928


October 23, 1984, Argued  
April 4, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 00566 PITTSBURGH 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Family No. M291 of 1979
Eichenlaub v. Eichenlaub, 33 Pa. D. & C.3d 59, 1983 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 86 (1983)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him for indirect criminal contempt of a protection order entered under the Protection from Abuse Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 10181 et seq., when he severely beat appellee wife during his fourth violation of a second protection order. Appellant protested the denial of a jury trial on constitutional grounds.


OVERVIEW: In considering appellant husband's challenge of the denial of a jury trial for the indirect criminal contempt charge he incurred after beating appellee wife, the court noted that while the Protection from Abuse Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 10181 et seq., had been upheld regarding double jeopardy and due process, it had not been challenged regarding the denial of a jury trial. The court asserted that since 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 10190 did not authorize a penalty of more than six months imprisonment, it did not violate appellant's right to a jury trial, unless the further authorization of a fine not to exceed $ 1,000 so enhanced the severity of the penalty as to render the offense of violating a protection order a serious, rather than a petty, offense. The court held that it did not, since summary disposition was essential to the effectiveness of the Protection from Abuse Act. The court concluded that the legislature did not clearly, palpably, and plainly violate appellant's constitutional rights. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. The denial of a jury trial did not violate the constitutional rights of appellant husband on an indirect criminal contempt charge for beating appellee wife in violation of a protection order. Summary disposition was essential to the effectiveness of a legislative act that was designed to curb abuse in the home.

Commonwealth v. Heck


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


341 Pa. Super. 183; 491 A.2d 212; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6824


September 4, 1984, Argued  
April 4, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition For Allowance of Appeal Granted February 5, 1986; (509 Pa. 535; 505 A.2d 251)


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 01457 Philadelphia 1983, APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE MAY 9, 1983 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF LANCASTER COUNTY, CRIMINAL NO. 1574 OF 1982


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), upon his conviction for vehicular homicide under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732. Defendant claimed there was insufficient evidence to support his conviction and that the statute was unconstitutional as violative of his due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of vehicular homicide under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732 after he made a left turn in front of an oncoming motorcyclist, causing his death. The court found that there was sufficient evidence to find that defendant should have known he was violating 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3322 , which proscribed illegal left turns, but found that the evidence was insufficient to establish any degree of culpability higher than ordinary negligence. The court held that the evidence was insufficient to prove that defendant's failure to apprehend the hazard in time was a "gross" deviation from a reasonable standard of care. The court ruled that all the evidence pointed to the conclusion that defendant was at most ordinarily negligent in causing the accident. The court reversed the conviction and held that due process of law, under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. 1, prohibited a conviction under § 3732 based on defendant's ordinary negligence in violating a traffic law, where the violation was neither knowing nor criminally negligent.


OUTCOME: The court found the evidence sufficient to support defendant's conviction for vehicular homicide, but reversed the judgment of sentence on the ground that the conviction unconstitutionally deprived defendant of due process of law by imposing criminal liability upon a showing of no more than ordinary negligence. The court determined that the vehicular homicide law predicated on ordinary negligence violated the constitution.

In re Gartley


No. 958 Pittsburgh, 1982


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


341 Pa. Super. 350; 491 A.2d 851; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6926


August 29, 1984, Argued  
April 4, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted September 16, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, July Term, 1982, Misc. No. 319.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted defendant's motion to quash the search warrant to obtain defendant's business records that defendant transferred to his attorney's possession for failure to specify the area to be searched with reasonable particularity.


OVERVIEW: The commonwealth executed a search warrant to obtain defendant's business records, which defendant had transferred to his attorney. The trial court granted defendant's motion to quash the warrant for failure to specify the area to be searched with reasonable particularity. Pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. IV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, a valid warrant must have particularly described the place to be searched. The court held that the warrant satisfied the constitutional particularity requirements. The court held that, although 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5916 and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5928 provided attorney-client and work doctrine privileges, the commonwealth was not seeking any of defendant's attorney's personal memoranda or notes regarding conversations with defendant. Therefore, defendant could not invoke the protection afforded by such privileges. The court held that defendant's attorney, not suspected of criminal activity, was not immune from a search authorized by a particularized warrant supported by probable cause. The court vacated the order of the trial court and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court vacated an order of the trial court, which granted defendant's motion to quash the search warrant for failure to specify the area to be searched with reasonable particularity. The court held that defendant's attorney was not suspected of criminal activity and was not immune from a search authorized by a particularized warrant supported by probable cause. Defendant could not invoke the attorney-client or work doctrine privileges.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) convicting appellant of murder in the first degree, theft by unlawful taking, and possession of instruments of crime.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. Appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree, theft by unlawful taking, and possession of instruments of crime. The court further held that the opinion of the trial judge thoroughly disposed of appellant's contention of insufficiency of the evidence.

Commonwealth v. Bryant


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


341 Pa. Super. 123; 491 A.2d 181; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7538


October 15, 1984, Submitted  
March 29, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 4, 1985.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, (Pennsylvania), which imposed concurrent sentences of two and one half to five years for convictions of firearms not to be carried without a license and former convict not to own a firearm.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's sentence entered for a convict not to possess a firearm, but reversed the conviction and vacated the judgment of sentence as to carrying a firearm without a license. The court found no need to remand for resentencing because by imposing concurrent terms of equal duration for two offenses catalogued as misdemeanors of the first degree, the trial court issued the maximum term possible for either offense. The court found that the prosecution failed to present a certificate from the commissioner of the state police documenting a search of his records and finding that appellant had not been issued a license by any authorized official within the commonwealth; therefore, appellant's conviction for carrying a firearm without a license had to be reversed. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence for a former convict possessing a firearm under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6105 because of testimony at trial by the arresting officer that appellant was in possession of a firearm, the court clerk's testimony of appellant's conviction of burglary, and the weapon in question was entered into evidence and reviewed by the jury.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of sentence as to carrying a firearm without a license because the prosecution failed to present a certificate from the commissioner of state police documenting a search of his records and finding that appellant had not been issued a license by any authorized official within the commonwealth. The court affirmed the sentence for a convict not to possess a firearm because sufficient evidence was produced at trial.

Viadock v. Nesbitt Memorial Hospital


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


339 Pa. Super. 437; 489 A.2d 240; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6001


October 18, 1984, Argued  
March 1, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 3547 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Order of November 16, 1982, Court of Common Pleas, Luzerne County, Civil Division at No. 19-C of 1981


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant department of public welfare sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant the right to intervene in a suit claiming damages arising from alleged medical malpractice.


OVERVIEW: Appellee beneficiary became ill, incurring substantial medical bills. Appellee instituted suit against two hospitals and twelve physicians for injuries incurred as a result of their alleged malpractice. Appellant department of public welfare, which had paid appellee's medical expenses and had provided funds her family during the pendency of the action, sought to intervene in order to recover the monies which had been expended. The trial court denied appellant the right to intervene, appellant challenged the decision, and the court reversed the trial court's order. The court held that the trial court abused its discretion because it relied on 40 P.S. § 1301.602, which was no longer viable due to the declaration that arbitration in medical malpractice was unconstitutional, and because it never considered appellant's right to intervene pursuant to the Support Law, 62 P.S. § 1974(a), the Fraud and Abuse Control Act, 62 P.S. § 1401 et. seq., and Pa. R. Civ. P. 2327. The court also held pursuant to 62 P.S. § 1409(b)(9), appellant should have been notified that a settlement agreement had been reached among the parties in the underlying suit.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order denying appellant department of public welfare the right to intervene and remanded to the trial court. The court held that where appellant had an interest, statute required that appellant be given notice and an opportunity to perfect and satisfy its lien prior to satisfaction of the judgment, award or settlement.

Walder v. Lobel


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


339 Pa. Super. 203; 488 A.2d 622; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5872


August 22, 1984, Argued  
February 15, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 01108 PHL 83, Appeal from the Order entered April 7, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 5394 August Term, 1973.

NO. 01114 PHL 83, Appeal from the Order entered April 7, 1983 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 5394 August Term, 1973.
Walder v. Lobel, 9 Phila. 134, 1983 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 50 (Pa. C.P., 1983)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a school and its owners and operators, appealed from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), granting defendants, a school and its owner, a new trial in a defamation action. Defendants appealed from the trial court's decision to deny their motion for judgment n.o.v. and their motion for arrest of judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a school and its owner, sent several letters, purportedly from students, to the Veterans Administration and to the Pennsylvania Association of Private School Administrators in order to discredit plaintiffs, a school and its owners and operators. The jury found defendants liable and awarded plaintiffs compensatory and punitive damages. The lower court granted defendants' motion for a new trial but denied their motions for judgment n.o.v. and for arrest of judgment. Both parties appealed. The court held that the instruction to the jury that the defendants bore burden of proving the truth of the defamatory communication was correct. Despite unrebutted testimony that, as a result of the letters, plaintiff school's income substantially decreased and it lost its accreditation, the court could not say, after reviewing the record, that the lower court reached a manifestly unreasonable judgement or abused its discretion in finding the verdict excessive. The court, therefore, modified the order granting a new trial by limiting the new trial to the issue of damages.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order granting defendants, a school and its owner, a new trial by limiting the new trial to the issue of damages. It remanded for a new trial solely on the issue of damages because the lower court correctly instructed the jury that the defendants bore burden of proving the truth of the defamatory communication.

 

482 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Turner


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


339 Pa. Super. 81; 488 A.2d 319; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5895


December 5, 1980, Submitted  
February 13, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1135 October Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County, at Nos. Misc. 79-00-3248 and MC 78-10-2309
Commonwealth v. Turner, 304 Pa. Super. 602, 450 A.2d 1043, 1982 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5107 (1982)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of carrying a firearm without a license in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6106.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of carrying a firearm without a license in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6106. He challenged his conviction, arguing that the language of the statute required appellee prosecution to prove that he was not concealing the weapon in his place of abode or fixed place of business. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that under the rules of statutory construction, appellant was required to put on an affirmative defense relating to his place of abode or fixed place of business. It was not an element of the crime to be proved by appellee. When interpreting statutes, the court had to look at the wording of the exception and its role in relation to the other words in the statute, whether in light of the situation prompting legislative action, the exception was essential to complete the general prohibition intended, whether the exception makes an excuse or justification for what would otherwise be criminal conduct, that was, set forth an affirmative defense, and whether the matter was peculiarly within appellant's knowledge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for carrying a firearm without a license, holding that appellee prosecution was not required to prove that appellant was not in his home or his fixed place of business at the time when he was carrying a concealed weapon without a license; rather, they were affirmative defenses to be proven by appellant.

Commonwealth v. Hassine


Superior Court of Pennsylvania

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant convict sought review of the judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which found appellant guilty of first degree murder, several attempted murders, and multiple counts of criminal conspiracy and criminal solicitation, and was sentenced to life imprisonment and various consecutive terms of imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convict was tried and convicted of first degree murder, several attempted murders, and multiple counts of criminal conspiracy and criminal solicitation for his participation in the shooting of three people. V and XIV were not violated by the impeachment use of appellant's pre-arrest silence and by testimony designed to rebut appellant's claim that he cooperated with police, and that the trial court had properly charged the jury on reasonable doubt and accomplice liability.
