Commonwealth v. Hernandez


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


339 Pa. Super. 32; 488 A.2d 293; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5640


August 15, 1984, Argued  
February 1, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 302 Harrisburg 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Perry County at No. 20 of 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Perry County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of involuntary manslaughter, homicide by vehicle, homicide by vehicle while driving under the influence, driving under the influence while impaired, driving under the influence-.10 percent, and operation of a vehicle with suspended or revoked license.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter, driving with a suspended license, homicide by vehicle, homicide by vehicle while driving under the influence, driving under the influence while impaired, and driving under the influence-.10 percent, which were violations of the New Drunk Driving Law (New Law) Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the New Law. On appeal, the court held that there was no violation of the separation of powers by the legislature in setting penalties. The mandatory sentencing provisions were not cruel and unusual and did not violate due process because they were rationally related to the legitimate public policy of protecting human life. The class of people subject to the mandatory provisions was not suspect, and the classification was rationally related to highway safety. The per se drunk at .10 percent rule was not vague and did not violate due process. The sentence enhancement provisions did not violate the prohibition against ex post facto laws. However, double jeopardy required the court to vacate the sentence for homicide by vehicle because appellant was also punished for involuntary manslaughter.


OUTCOME: The court denied appellant's constitutional challenges to the New Drunk Driving Law, under which he was convicted and sentenced because they did not violate due process and were rationally related to public policy. However, pursuant to the prohibition against double jeopardy, the court vacated the judgment of sentence for homicide by vehicle because appellant had also been sentenced for involuntary manslaughter.

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


338 Pa. Super. 458; 487 A.2d 1372; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5675

January 30, 1985, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 7, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2421 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Montgomery County, No. 81-22360.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant construction corporation challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, (Pennsylvania), which denied its petition to strike or open a personal injury judgment that was recovered in West Virginia and transferred to Pennsylvania, pursuant to the Uniform Enforcement of Foreign Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4306.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, husband and wife, filed an action against appellant construction corporation in West Virginia to recover for personal injuries sustained in an automobile accident. Although service was obtained, appellant did not answer the complaint. After appellees collected in part on the default judgment, they filed a praecipe for judgment in Pennsylvania, pursuant to the Uniform Enforcement of Foreign Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4306. The trial court denied appellant's motion to open or strike the judgment. The court affirmed and held that the West Virginia court clearly had jurisdiction to determine the integrity of its own judgment, and it upheld the finality of appellees' judgment. The judgment was not fraudulently obtained, and there was neither a factual nor a legal basis for relief. The West Virginia determination was res judicata with respect to the validity of the judgment. The court held that judgment was not offensive to due process and was entitled to full faith and credit in Pennsylvania. The court remanded the case to the trial court to enter an order amending the judgment to reflect the partial satisfaction that was effected in West Virginia.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant construction corporation's petition to strike or open a personal injury judgment that was recovered in West Virginia and transferred to Pennsylvania. The court held that the judgment was entitled to full faith and credit under the Uniform Enforcement of Foreign Judgments Act because there was no basis for relief from it.

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


337 Pa. Super. 548; 487 A.2d 396; 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5839


May 7, 1984, Argued  
January 11, 1985, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 00015 Pittsburgh, 1982, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Lawrence County at Nos. 183, 183B, C. D of 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to two and one-half to five years, after a jury trial found him guilty of statutory rape, incest, and attempted involuntary deviate sexual intercourse with respect to his 13 year old daughter. Defendant challenged the lower court's application of Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found defendant guilty of statutory rape, incest, and attempted involuntary deviate sexual intercourse with respect to his 13-year-old daughter. Defendant was convicted on the strength of his daughter's testimony. Defendant challenged the lower court's application of Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104, which prevented him from introducing evidence of his daughter's prior sexual conduct with one of her brothers for the purpose of showing bias. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the case to the lower court for a hearing in accordance with the opinion. The court held that Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Law could not be used to exclude relevant evidence showing witness' bias or attacking credibility. The court held that insofar as the Rape Shield Law purported to prohibit the admission of evidence which may logically demonstrate a witness' bias, interest or prejudice or which properly attacked the witness' credibility, it unconstitutionally infringed upon defendant's right of confrontation under the U.S. Const. amend. VI, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case. The court held that insofar as the Rape Shield Law purported to prohibit the admission of evidence which may logically demonstrate a witness' bias, interest or prejudice or which properly attacked the witness' credibility, it unconstitutionally infringed upon defendant's right of confrontation.

 

488 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Grady


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


337 Pa. Super. 174; 486 A.2d 962; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 7187


September 20, 1984, Submitted  
December 28, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 339 Harrisburg, 1983, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Dauphin County, No. 758 C.D. 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), that convicted him of driving while under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731 and sentenced him to a minimum term of 30 days imprisonment in addition to a fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for a second offense of driving while under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731 and sentenced to a minimum term of 30 days imprisonment in addition to a fine. The court affirmed the judgment of sentence because the recidivist section of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(e)(1)(ii) was not violative of constitutional provisions proscribing ex post facto laws. The court held that the enactment of § 3731(e)(1)(ii) did not make criminal an act that had previously been lawful and it did not alter the rules of evidence to require less or different testimony in order to convict. The statutory amendment, which provided a heavier sentence for a second offense, was not constitutionally infirm merely because it required a sentencing court to consider a conviction that had occurred before the new law went into effect. As of the effective date of § 3731(e)(1)(ii) defendant was on notice as to what would happen if he were again convicted of driving while intoxicated. The court held that the penalty defendant faced did not result from the legislation, but rather from his own acts that took place after the effective date of the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sentenced defendant, on a second offense of driving while under the influence of alcohol, to 30 days imprisonment in addition to a fine because the recidivist section of the offense of driving while under the influence of alcohol was not violative of constitutional provisions proscribing ex post facto laws.

Norris v. Wood


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


336 Pa. Super. 305; 485 A.2d 817; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6972


August 23, 1984, Argued  
December 14, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 01046 Philadelphia 1983, Appeal of Thomasine Norris from the Judgment entered March 24, 1983, of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, Law, for the County of Philadelphia at No. 1601, September Term, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the partial summary judgment order by the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, for the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) in favor of appellee the Pennsylvania Assigned Claims Plan. Appellant contended that the trial court's order was based on a constitutionally defective statute.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's father was killed when the uninsured car he was driving was struck by another vehicle. Appellant filed an action against appellee, the Pennsylvania Assigned Claims Plan, who counterclaimed that it was entitled under 40 P.S. § 1009.501(1985) to recover the money it paid to appellant, the owner of the uninsured vehicle. Appellant contended that because the statute required her to pay the damages caused by the accident, and suspension of her license and was subject to prosecution if she failed to pay, the pay-back provision violated her right to equal protection under the law. When no suspect class or fundamental right existed, the statute must be examined to find whether a reasonable relation existed between its classification and the legislative goal. The court affirmed because here, the object of barring driver's licenses to those who do not pay judgments was to prevent future occurrences of uncompensated injury from burdening the Plan. The classification of owners into insured, underinsured and uninsured accomplishes this goal. The scheme prevented those who force the Plan to pay out benefits from repeating their acts.


OUTCOME: The court held that the classification in the statute was reasonably related to the legislative goal. Therefore, it affirmed the holding below that no violation of equal protection exists.

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 28, 1985. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 18, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1499 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from Judgment Entered May 12, 1982 Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, Centre County, No. 1980-600


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pedestrian sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas, Centre County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's motion for new trial and entered judgment for appellee motorist in a suit for damages as a result of accidental injury.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pedestrian filed suit against appellee motorist for injuries sustained when appellee ran over appellant while appellant was walking across the street. The trial court denied appellant's motion for new trial and entered judgment for appellee, and the court reversed and remanded holding that the trial court erred in its charge to the jury and such error required reversal. The court further held that the admission of appellant's blood alcohol level was proper when it showed his unfitness in crossing the street. The court held that the trial court's jury charge as to the presumption of being under the influence of alcohol as set out in 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. 1547(d)(3) was erroneous and caused sufficient prejudice to appellant so as to warrant a new trial.


OUTCOME: Order denying appellant pedestrian's motion for new trial and entering judgment for appellee motorist reversed and remanded for new trial because although evidence of appellant's intoxication was relevant and admissible as to his unfitness in crossing the street, the trial court's jury charge as to the presumption under the law that he was intoxicated was erroneous and so prejudicial as to require reversal.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged an order of the Court Of Common Pleas of Bucks (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgement for appellee father, finding that appellant's action was barred by the statute of limitations in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6704(b), arguing that the statute violated her equal protection rights, prohibition against special laws, the Equal Rights Amendment, and that appellant was estopped to deny paternity by contributing support.

Commonwealth v. Waters


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


334 Pa. Super. 513; 483 A.2d 855; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6389


June 11, 1984, Argued  
October 12, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 28, 1984. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 14, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 00433 Philadelphia 1983, APPEAL FROM THE JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE JANUARY 25, 1983 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF CUMBERLAND COUNTY, CRIMINAL NO. 893 1981


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania) for the offenses of first degree murder and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse.


OVERVIEW: In challenging his convictions for murder and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, appellant claimed that the trial court erred in failing to suppress his confession. The court applied the totality of circumstances analysis and held that the lower court properly denied appellant's motion to suppress the confession. The court found there was no evidence that appellant was subjected to physical or psychological abuse. Both appellant and his father were informed of appellant's Miranda rights on two occasions prior to their private conference out of the presence of the officers. Both appellant and his father indicated that they wanted to waive appellant's rights and both agreed that the appellant would talk to the police without his father being present. During the course of questioning that followed, appellant on numerous occasions indicated that he did not object to talking to the officers alone without his father being present. Appellant then confessed to the murder. The court held that it was clear from the totality of the circumstances that appellant's confession was knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily made. The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.


OUTCOME: Appellant's judgment of sentence for murder and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse was affirmed because his confession was knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily made, because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in failing to transfer the matter to the juvenile court or in failing to declare a mistrial, and because the age classification of the involuntary deviate sexual intercourse statute was not arbitrary.

 

494 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Fernandez


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


333 Pa. Super. 279; 482 A.2d 567; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6077


June 27, 1984, Submitted  
September 21, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1860 Philadelphia 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of June 11, 1982, by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, No. 1879-81.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal challenged a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) after he was convicted of two counts of murder in the second degree, burglary, and criminal attempt of robbery.


OVERVIEW: During appellant criminal's trial, appellant was ordered to shave his beard to facilitate an in court identification. Also, appellant's request for a mistrial, and to introduce evidence of an alibi after the prosecution has closed its case-in-chief were denied. After conviction for murder, burglary, and criminal attempt of robbery, appellant challenged the judgment of sentence. The court affirmed. Appellant's right against self-incrimination, Pa. Const. art. I, § 9, as afforded by U.S. Const. amend V, was not violated by the order to shave. The Fifth Amendment excluded only evidence which was testimonial in nature, not that which was demonstrative or physical. Further, the interest of justice was served by discouragement of appellant's attempt to disguise himself. After an innocuous reference by a co-defendant indicated that appellant was involved in drug activity, a request for mistrial was correctly refused in light of a curative instruction. Appellant's attempt to introduce alibi testimony in the middle of the sixth day of trial was precluded by Pa. R. Crim. P. 305C, which required notice. Justice demanded exclusion because of the prejudice to the prosecution.


OUTCOME: Appellant criminal's judgment of sentence for murder, burglary, and criminal attempt of robbery was affirmed as his right against self-incrimination was not violated by order to shave for identification purposes at trial. Moreover, a request for mistrial was properly refused when an innocuous reference to distinct crime was made but an effective curative instruction was given and refusal to allow alibi defense was correct when untimely made.

Commonwealth v. Dear


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6142



September 7, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] No. 191 Harrisburg 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Dauphin County at No. 1630 C.D. 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which entered a conviction against him for rape and robbery.


OVERVIEW: According to the victim, defendant approached her at a bar and asked her to have sex with him. She refused. Subsequently, while the victim was walking outside, defendant forcibly raped her at gunpoint. He also took $ 100 from her. The medical evidence was consistent with the victim's account and a pubic hair belonging to defendant was found on the victim. At trial, defendant testified that he knew the victim to be a prostitute and that she had solicited him. The trial court denied a defense motion to offer into evidence the victim's convictions for criminal solicitation in prostitution, which all occurred in the same generally area as the alleged rape. Defendant was found guilty of rape and robbery. On appeal, defendant argued that his right to confront the witness against him was violated by exclusion of the victim's prior sexual history because it was probative on the issue of consent. The court thought that it was inadmissible under Pennsylvania's Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104, because it was evidence of sexual conduct with third persons. The court also denied defendant's motion for a new trial. His convictions for rape and robbery were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for rape and robbery because it determined that the trial court correctly excluded evidence of the victim's convictions for criminal solicitation in prostitution because Pennsylvania's Rape Shield law barred the admissibility of a rape victim's sexual conduct with third persons.

American Booksellers Asso. v. Rendell


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


332 Pa. Super. 537; 481 A.2d 919; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5831


September 7, 1983, Argued  
August 24, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 8, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 863 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Final Decree in the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, of Philadelphia County, December Term, 1980, No. 3035.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant publishers, wholesalers, and retailers challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which determined that the state obscenity statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903(a)(1), was constitutionally sound and denied appellants' request for declaratory and permanent injunctive relief. Appellants contended that the trial court erred because the statute was impermissibly overbroad and vague.


OVERVIEW: Appellant publishers, wholesalers, and retailers challenged an order from a trial court that upheld the state's obscenity statute against constitutional attack. Appellants had filed an action seeking to have 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903(a)(1) declared unconstitutional on the grounds that compliance with the statute necessitated the exclusion of all minors from store premises, or required that they display solely materials suitable for children. Appellants argued that the trial court erred in upholding the statute's constitutionality because the terms "harmful to minors," "display," "minor," and "as part of the general public or otherwise" were so vague that they gave no notice of the prohibited conduct. Prior to the action, none of the appellants been charged or threatened with prosecution. After determining that appellants had standing, the appellate court affirmed the order upholding the constitutionality of the statute because its language was sufficiently specific to provide notice of proscribed conduct. The appellate court held that the fact that more precise language might have been chosen did not render the statute unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The order upholding the constitutionality of the statute was affirmed because appellants failed to satisfy the heavy burden borne by those challenging a statute as overbroad or vague. The court held that carefully drawn restrictions on the sale or distribution of materials not obscene for adults to juveniles was allowed, and the mere fact that appellants might engage in self-censorship did not require invalidation of the statute.

Commonwealth v. Scatena


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


332 Pa. Super. 415; 481 A.2d 855; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5615


January 11, 1984, Argued  
August 3, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted November 20, 1984. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 20, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: NO. 1723 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal, at No. 655, 655-A and 655-B of 1980, NO. 1724 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal, at No. 656 and 656-A of 1980, NO. 1725 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal, at No. 657 and 657-A of 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, father and son, appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) that convicted them of risking catastrophe, public nuisances, and violations of the Clean Streams Law, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 691.1 et seq. Defendant father was also convicted of three counts of conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, father and son, owned a service station. For a fee, defendants allowed an oil company to dispose of its industrial waste through a sewer hole on defendants' property. The waste eventually ended up in a local river. Defendants were convicted of risking catastrophe, public nuisances, and violations of the Clean Streams Law, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 691.1 et seq. Defendant father was also convicted of three counts of conspiracy. On appeal, the court vacated the risking catastrophe convictions, but affirmed the remaining convictions. There was insufficient evidence to convict defendants for risking catastrophe under 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3302(b). There was no evidence that the chemicals were inherently dangerous or that defendants' actions would cause widespread injury. The court also vacated defendant father's sentence for conspiracy and remanded for resentencing because he could only be found guilty of but one charge of conspiracy. Pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 906, defendant father's conduct was designed to commit the same crime.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendants' conviction for risking catastrophe because there was insufficient evidence that chemicals could cause widespread injury or were inherently harmful. The court vacated the sentence imposed for defendant father's conspiracy convictions and remanded for resentencing because he could only be found guilty of one conspiracy.

 

498 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Crystal D.R.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


331 Pa. Super. 501; 480 A.2d 1146; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5481


November 15, 1983, Argued  
July 20, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 7, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: NO. 1658 PHILA. 1983, Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Orphans


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania), which terminated their parental rights and awarded appellee foster parents custody of the minor child in appellees' action that sought termination of appellants' parental rights to enable appellees to adopt the child.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parents sought review after their parental rights were terminated on the petition of appellee foster parents. The court reversed and held that appellees did not have the statutory authority to file such a petition. The court held that 23 P.S. § 2512(a) clearly limited those that could petition for the termination of parental rights to a natural parent, a state agency, or an individual having custody or standing in loco parentis to the child. The court reasoned that in a foster care situation, the foster parents did not stand in loco parentis to the child because the state agency that was involved had responsibility for the actual care and custody of the child. The court reviewed the legislative intent and found that the purpose of the language was to clarify that the relationship between the foster parents and the child was subordinate both to the relationship between the agency and the child and to the relationship between the child and the child's parents, and that this structure helped insure that the child's best interests were considered, and that the natural parents and foster parents did not become rivals for the child.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order terminating appellant parents' rights and remanded the case holding that appellee foster parents did not have standing to file a petition for termination of parental rights when the applicable statute clearly limited those that had such authority, the foster parents were not included in the statutory language, and the legislative intent was clear that foster parents should not have such a right.

Commonwealth v. Croll


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


331 Pa. Super. 107; 480 A.2d 266; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5377


December 1, 1983, Argued  
July 6, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 436 Pittsburgh, 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County at C.A.B. No. 291 of 1981, Book 72, Page 77.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas, Butler County (Pennsylvania), following appellant's conviction of sales of obscene material. Appellant contested the propriety of the sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by the trial court of sales of obscene material and fined $ 10,000 as a condition of probation. The court affirmed the conviction but vacated the judgment of sentence and remanded for resentencing. The court stated that the statute under which appellant was charged was not unconstitutional. The court held that the Pennsylvania Constitution afforded no greater protection from prosecution for the distribution and sale of obscene materials than did the United States Constitution. The court also held that the evidence was legally sufficient to support the conviction. The court found that proof was adequate to show that appellant knew the contents of the films he sold. The court stated that the doors of the viewing booths clearly provided notice of the nature of the films for sale in the store where appellant worked in a supervisory capacity. The court concluded that with only a few facts before him and without inquiring into appellant's ability to pay, the trial judge imposed a substantial fine. Accordingly, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9726 (c)-(d), the court vacated the sentence and remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of appellant of sales of obscene material but vacated the judgment of sentence and remanded for resentencing. The court held that with only a few facts before him and without inquiring into appellant's ability to pay, the trial judge imposed a substantial fine. Accordingly, pursuant to statute, the court vacated the sentence and remanded for resentencing.

Commonwealth v. Rawls


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


328 Pa. Super. 469; 477 A.2d 540; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4958


February 3, 1984, Submitted  
June 8, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 3596 Philadelphia 1982, APPEAL FROM THE PCHA DECEMBER 16, 1982 IN THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, CRIMINAL NO. 208-212 MAY TERM 1977


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied his request for relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9551. Appellant argued that he was denied full appellate rights and the right to the representation of competent counsel on a prior direct appeal from a judgment of sentence for second-degree murder, robbery, burglary, and conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of second-degree murder, robbery, burglary, and conspiracy. His conviction was upheld on direct appeal. He sought relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9551, stating that his court-appointed counsel abandoned him after the court affirmed the judgment of sentence on direct appeal, and that he was denied his right to seek further review by such ineffective assistance. Appellant had not sought state supreme court review and was not seeking it under the PCHA. Instead, he sought to advance two new claims to the court: (1) that the felony murder rule under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502(b) as applied to him was unconstitutional, and (2) that the elements of the jury trial waiver colloquy should have been expanded to inform him of the unique powers of a jury in a homicide prosecution. The court affirmed the decision of the PCHA court because numerous courts had refused to strike down the felony murder rule and counsel's failure to challenge its constitutionality could not be equated with ineffectiveness. The court dealt summarily with the colloquy issue because appellant conceded compliance with applicable law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that had denied appellant post conviction relief under the post conviction relief law because his attorneys' failure to pursue a constitutional challenge to the felony murder rule, which had been sustained numerous times, was not ineffective assistance of counsel, and because appellant conceded that the jury trial waiver colloquy in his case had been in compliance with applicable law.

Commonwealth v. Beauford


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


327 Pa. Super. 253; 475 A.2d 783; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4440


February 17, 1983, Argued  
April 13, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 18, 1984. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted November 29, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 3351 Philadelphia 1981, No. 247 Philadelphia 1982, No. 1136 Philadelphia 1982, No. 1763 Philadelphia 1982, No. 1798 Philadelphia 1982, No. 2666 Philadelphia 1982, APPEALS FROM JUDGMENTS OF SENTENCE, COURT OF COMMON PLEAS, DELAWARE COUNTY, CRIMINAL DIVISION AT NOS. 1198-81, 1523-81, 1197-81, 1199-81, 1521-81 and 1522-81.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which convicted them of lotteries and gambling in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5512 and 5513.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of lotteries and gambling in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5512 and 5513, respectively. Defendants appealed from their judgments, contending that their U.S. Const. amend. IV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 rights to privacy were violated by law enforcement agencies' use of pen registers and Dial Number Recorders (DNR) without a court order that authorized such use. The court consolidated the appeals, reversed the convictions, and remanded for further proceedings. The Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5726, stated that it was not unlawful for any investigative or law enforcement officer to use a pen register under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5704(5). The difference between pen registers as defined in § 5704(5), and DNRs, was immaterial. However, though the United States Supreme Court held that the use of a pen register did not violate U.S. Const. amend. IV, Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 extended greater protection. Thus, defendants and the persons with whom they telephonically communicated with had an expectation of privacy which law enforcement agencies could not violate absent a court order entered upon a showing of probable cause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments that convicted defendants of lotteries and gambling and remanded for further proceedings, as their respective rights to privacy were violated by the use of pen register and Dial Number Recorders without a court order by various law enforcement agencies' in violation of the Pennsylvania Constitution.

 

504 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Brown


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


327 Pa. Super. 505; 476 A.2d 381; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4447


December 15, 1983, Argued  
April 13, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1498 PHILA. 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal No. 2471-82


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of burglary, criminal trespass, and criminal conspiracy on the ground that he was denied his constitutional right to counsel by the trial court's refusal to appoint counsel to represent appellant at trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was tried without counsel after the public defender refused to represent him. The appellate court vacated the conviction, holding that appellant's right to counsel had been denied. There had been no proper determination of appellant's eligibility for appointed counsel. The trial court relied on the public defender's assertions that appellant was able to afford counsel based on his income and assets and the fact that he had made bail. The court pointed out that, after rent, appellant's daily net income was $ 10.00 and there was no evidence that his asset, a car, had any value. Also, there was evidence that appellant's mother had posted his bail. The court questioned the public defender's and trial court's beliefs that appellant would have been able to hire an attorney given his limited income and assets. There had been no guidelines used in determining appellant's eligibility and no consideration of appellant's other liabilities. The fact that appellant appeared for trial without counsel did not constitute a waiver where appellant stated that he was unable to afford an attorney. The case was remanded for a thorough determination of appellant's ability to afford counsel.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's conviction and remanded for a new trial. The court held that appellant's right to counsel had been denied. The trial court had not adequately determined whether appellant was or was not eligible for court appointed counsel and its reliance on the assertion of the Public Defender that appellant was ineligible for its services was unfounded.

Grube v. Troncelliti


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


326 Pa. Super. 339; 473 A.2d 1379; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4221


February 16, 1983, Argued  
March 23, 1984, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 242 Philadelphia 1982, Appeal from the Judgment entered of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil, at No. 80-11172


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil (Pennsylvania), in favor of appellee doctor, and the denial of appellant's motion to read into the record the findings of fact and the decision of the arbitrators as provided by the Pennsylvania Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P.S. § 1301.510, in a medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: The trial court entered judgment denying appellant's motion for new trial and motion in arrest of judgment. Appellant had instituted a medical malpractice action by filing a complaint in accordance with the Pennsylvania Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301.101 et seq. The arbitrators decided unanimously in appellant's favor. Appellee doctor took an appeal de novo and a verdict was returned in his favor. The trial court denied appellant's motion to read into the record the findings of fact and the decision of the arbitrators as provided by 40 P.S. § 1301.510. On appeal, appellant contended the trial court erred in so doing. The court held that in the case of § 1301.510, the legislature, by inclusion of a severability clause, had expressed its intent that the constitutional provisions should stand even as their unconstitutional adjunct, 40 P.S. § 1301.309, had fallen. In light of the great weight which must be afforded the expressed intention of the legislature and because § 1301.510 was capable of separation in fact, it remained valid. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment below in favor of appellee doctor on the grounds that the trial court erred in not permitting appellant to place the findings of the arbitration panel before the jury and that the error necessitated a new trial. The court vacated the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case for a new trial.

In re Adoption of Michael J.C.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


326 Pa. Super. 143; 473 A.2d 1021; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4077


September 19, 1983, Argued  
March 2, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 30, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1501 Philadelphia 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Delaware County, No. 62 of 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural mother sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which terminated her parental rights concerning her minor child pursuant to 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2511(a)(2), after denying her request for visitation and rejecting her withdrawal of consent.


OVERVIEW: Parental rights of appellant natural mother were terminated by the trial court pursuant to 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2511(a)(2). Appellant argued that she had withdrawn her consent to the adoption within the six-month period required by the statute, but the court found that while the general rule was that a parent's consent was required, the trial court relied on § 2511(a)(2), which did not have the six-month requirement. The court found that parental incapacity must have been shown, and that such incapacity could not have been remedied. The court found that clear and convincing evidence was required to prove this inability to remedy, and the evidence presented by witnesses at trial did not meet that standard. The court reversed and remanded the case, holding that the requirements for termination as contained in § 2511(a), regarding proof of incapacity, must have been met before the termination could have occurred. The court also remanded to the trial court for a determination whether appellant should have had custody of the child pending the resolution of the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the termination of parental rights of appellant natural mother, holding that clear and convincing evidence was not presented, and the termination could not have occurred if there was a reasonable possibility that appellant's incapacity to care for her child could have been remedied.

Gravinese v. Johns-Manville Corp.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


324 Pa. Super. 432; 471 A.2d 1233; 1984 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3766; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P9977


April 25, 1983, Argued  
January 20, 1984, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 23, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2504 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County, No. 4052 (131) Case No. 67 January Term, 1979.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant personal representative challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee asbestos manufacturer's motion for summary judgment in appellant's wrongful death and survival actions. Appellant asserted that the actions, filed seven years after decedent's death, were not barred by the statute of limitations because of maritime law, laches, the discovery rule, and estoppel.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a grant of summary judgment that held that the wrongful death and survival actions brought by appellant personal representative of decedent's estate, were barred by the statutes of limitations. Appellant's husband was exposed to asbestos products while working as a pipe insulator aboard U.S. Navy ships both in dry dock and on navigable waters. Appellant filed the actions seven years after her husband's death and asserted that she did not discover that asbestos was the cause of his cancer until less than one year ago. The court held that admiralty law and the doctrine of laches did not apply to appellant's actions when her husband was not engaged in activity that bore a significant relationship to traditional maritime activity. The court held that the discovery rule was inapplicable to wrongful death and survival actions. The court held that appellee asbestos manufacturers were not estopped from raising the defense of statute of limitations when they had not caused appellant to delay her action through fraud or concealment. The court concluded that the plain language of the statutes, 12 P.S. § 34 and 12 P.S. § 1603, barred appellant's actions as untimely.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee asbestos manufacturers. The court held that maritime law and laches did not apply to appellant's actions when her husband, who worked on ships, was not engaged in traditional maritime activity. The court held that the discovery rule did not apply to wrongful death or survival actions and that appellees were not estopped from raising the defense of the statute of limitations.

Estate of Tower


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


323 Pa. Super. 235; 470 A.2d 568; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4537


April 13, 1983, Argued  
December 23, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 14, 1984. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted August 22, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 473 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from the Decree of the Court of Common Pleas, Orphans' Court Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 199 July Term, 1890.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptees, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found that the testator's manifest intent was to limit the class of income beneficiaries to the blood descendants of deceased grandchildren.


OVERVIEW: In connection with the audit of a trust, appellant adoptees sought to participate in the share of income that had been paid to their adoptive father during his lifetime. Prior adjudication of this issue by the courts had held that the testator intended, at the time he wrote his will, to include only blood relatives in the class of those who would share in the income. Appellants argued that the prior decisions were not res judicata and despite any lack of ambiguity in the decedent's will, subsequent court decisions dictated that adopted children's rights were equal to those of natural children. The court held that it agreed with the prior decisions finding that when the testator drafted his will he used unequivocal language to limit his bounty to his blood line and no other. The court held the prior decisions were res judicata.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court which denied appellant adoptees, participation in the income distribution of an estate as heirs at law of their adoptive father. The court held inter alia, that the testator had manifested a clear intent to limit income to his blood line when he drew his will, and subsequent changes of law concerning rules of construction did not affect that intent.

 

510 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


DiSerafino v. Bucyrus-Erie Corp.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


323 Pa. Super. 247; 470 A.2d 574; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4543


October 27, 1982, Argued  
December 23, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 9, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1415 PHILADELPHIA 1981, No. 1202 Philadelphia 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division of Delaware County at No. 75-12945.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, decedent's widow and children, appealed an order by the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which granted a motion by defendant employer to mold a verdict that had been rendered in favor of plaintiffs in a wrongful death action brought under the Pennsylvania Wrongful Death and Survival Act.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, decedent's widow and children, filed an action against defendant manufacturer under the Pennsylvania Wrongful Death and Survival Act. The manufacturer joined the decedent's employer as an addition defendant. The jury found in favor of plaintiffs but only as against defendant employer, awarding $ 275,000 under the Wrongful Death Act and $ 190,000 under the Survival Act. The trial judge denied defendant employer's motion for judgment n.o.v., but granted its motion to mold the verdict. Verdict was entered in favor of defendant employer and against plaintiffs. Plaintiffs' motion for a new trial was denied. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Act barred plaintiffs' recovery under the Wrongful Death Act. Decedent failed to give written notice of any refusal to accept the protection of the workmen's compensation act, thus agreeing to look to the it as an exclusive remedy. Plaintiffs were also bound by that agreement. Plaintiffs conceded that recovery under the Survival Act was barred. Plaintiffs failed to make specific exception to an alleged in error in a jury charge, thus review was waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant employer's motion to mold a verdict in its favor and against plaintiffs, decedent's widow and children. Monetary damages that were awarded to plaintiffs under the Pennsylvania Wrongful Death and Survival Act were barred by the Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Act.

Miller v. Gay


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


323 Pa. Super. 466; 470 A.2d 1353; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4507


February 23, 1982, Argued  
December 16, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 27, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1290 Philadelphia 1981, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadephia County, Civil Division, at No. 4654 December 1980
Miller v. Gay, 6 Phila. 294, 1981 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 44 (Pa. C.P., 1981)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her complaint on the grounds of "forum non conveniens" in her action seeking damages for injuries she suffered in an automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured was a passenger in a vehicle driven by appellee driver, a resident of the commonwealth, during an auto accident that occurred in the state of her residence. Appellant brought an action in the lower court seeking damages her injuries. Appellee filed preliminary objections to the complaint alleging the complaint should have been dismissed. The lower court dismissed appellant's complaint on forum non conveniens grounds. The court affirmed the trial court's order, holding that the law of appellant's domicile governed the case and because there was no significant relationship to the commonwealth that would call for application of its law rather than that of appellant's state of domicile. The court found that dismissal of appellant's complaint should not have rest on forum non conveniens grounds because it resulted in her being unable to bring the action elsewhere. The court concluded that appellant's sole connection with the commonwealth was that appellee was a resident, that certainty, predictability, and uniformity of result would be aided by the application of the law of appellant's state, and that there was no difficulty in the application of that law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, holding that the law of appellant injured's domicile, governed the case and because there was no significant relationship to the commonwealth that would call for application of its law rather than that of appellant's state of domicile.

Commonwealth v. Boyd


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


320 Pa. Super. 560; 467 A.2d 855; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4193


September 10, 1980, Argued  
November 4, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1882 October Term 1979, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at Nos. 500 and 501 August Term 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of recklessly endangering another person and aggravated assault, and sentenced him to five years probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of recklessly endangering another person and aggravated assault and sentenced to five years probation. The court vacated the order that convicted defendant, holding that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5104(c) was unconstitutional. The court held that the decision to grant or deny a defense request for a non-jury trial must be made only by the trial court, therefore a statute giving the state the same right to jury trial as the accused precluded the trial court from exercising the discretion conferred by Pa. R. Crim. P. 1101. On remand, the court held that in deciding whether to allow a defendant to waive his right to a jury trial, the trial court should take into account all relevant considerations. The court reversed and remanded the cause for a new trial with instructions that if defendant again stated his intention to waive a jury trial, the trial court should proceed under Pa. R. Crim. P. 1101, to decide whether to approve the waiver.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order from the trial court that convicted defendant of recklessly endangering another person and aggravated assault, sentencing him to five years probation. The court held that the trial court had discretion in deciding whether to allow defendant to waive his right to jury trial. A conflicting statute giving the state the same right was held unconstitutional.

Commonwealth v. Middleton


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


320 Pa. Super. 533; 467 A.2d 841; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4168


February 24, 1983, Argued  
October 28, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 23, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1233 Pittsburgh, 1981, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Venango County, Criminal Division, at S.D. No. 20-A of 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Venango County (Pennsylvania), convicting him of second-degree felony murder and sentencing him to life in prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second-degree felony murder and sentenced to life in prison. His motions for a new trial and an arrest of judgment were denied by the trial court. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that defendant failed to preserve his attack on the constitutionality of the felony-murder statute for appellate review. The court further found that defendant's sentence of life imprisonment did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment. Finally, the court concluded that the trial court properly denied defendant's demurrer because the evidence was sufficient to establish his guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. There was no doubt that there was a conspiratorial design to commit the underlying felony, the robbery of the victim, of which defendant was a part. Defendant was the actor in the initial robbery attempt; he carried the gun used in that attempt; and after the attempt was temporarily thwarted, he engaged in the discussion of continuing the robbery and accompanied others for that purpose. It did not matter whether defendant anticipated that the victim would be killed in furtherance of the conspiracy.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of second-degree felony murder and sentencing him to life in prison was affirmed because defendant failed to preserve his attack on the constitutionality of the felony-murder statute for appellate review. The court also found that defendant's sentence of life imprisonment did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

Commonwealth v. O'Bryant


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


320 Pa. Super. 231; 467 A.2d 14; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4104


June 11, 1980, Argued  
October 14, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 27, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2451 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 818-819 February Term, 1978


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the judgment of sentence of the Criminal Division of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) for robbery and conspiracy. Appellant argued that the lower court erred in denying a request made by him to be tried without a jury. Appellant also argued that certain items of evidence should have been suppressed for various reasons.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convict was found guilty, by a jury, of charges of robbery and conspiracy. The incident that led to his arrest occurred when a restaurant was robbed at gunpoint by two men, one of whom wore a ski mask and was subsequently identified as appellant. Appellant argued that the lower court erred in denying a request made by him to be tried without a jury pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. Proc. 1101. The lower court denied the request was denied because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5104(c) gave to appellee prosecution a right to jury trial equal to that of a defendant. Appellant also argued that the evidence of photo identifications, the gun seized from his mother's home, and his statement to the police following his arrest, should have been suppressed. The court noted that the statute giving the prosecutor an equal right to a jury trial had been declared unconstitutional by the state supreme court and therefore reversed and remanded for a new trial on this basis alone. The court affirmed the trial court's prior evidentiary rulings and found that the trial court had properly refused appellant's request to suppress the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence for appellant's conviction of robbery and conspiracy and remanded. The court found, based on the state supreme court's opinion, that a statute giving appellee prosecution an equal right to a trial by jury was unconstitutional, that the trial court's denial of appellant convict's request to waive jury trial was improperly denied. Appellant's suppression arguments were properly denied.

CASE SUMMARY:

Estate of Hoffman


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


320 Pa. Super. 113; 466 A.2d 1087; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4111


May 31, 1983, Argued  
October 14, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1716 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Orphans' Court Division, of Montgomery County, No. 80159.
Hoffman Estate, 13 Pa. D. & C.3d 611, 1980 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 543 (1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant daughter sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Orphans' Court Division, of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) which ruled that appellant failed to establish by clear and convincing evidence under 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2107 (1978) that she was the natural daughter of the testator's deceased brother, born out of wedlock, and entitled to a share of the residuary estate.


OVERVIEW: A testator's nearest surviving relative was appellee sister. Appellant daughter was born out of wedlock to the sister-in-law of the testator's deceased brother (deceased) and appellant claimed that she was entitled to one-half of the residuary. Appellant was taken into the deceased's home shortly after her birth and was there raised, and insurance records named the deceased as her father. The trial court found that the evidence failed to establish by clear and convincing evidence that appellant was the deceased's daughter. Appellant sought reversal. The court affirmed, holding that while 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2107 (1978), allowing an out-of-wedlock child to establish paternity, did not apply to appellant because the deceased died prior to the statute's effective date, but appellant could establish her claim by proving that she was the deceased's daughter; while appellant presented evidence of a close relationship with the deceased, proof of a biological relationship was diluted by her failure to call her mother to identify the deceased as the father, and such failure raised an inference that her testimony would have bee unfavorable to appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment which found that appellant daughter failed to prove by clear and convincing evidence that she was entitled to a share of the residuary estate as the testator's deceased brother's daughter, because appellant's failure to call her mother as a witness raised an inference that the mother's testimony would have been unfavorable.

Tierney v. Pennsylvania Assigned Claims Plan


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


319 Pa. Super. 299; 466 A.2d 168; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3951


April 13, 1983, Argued  
September 23, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 3005 PHILADELPHIA, 1981, Appeal from an Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County, No. 698 June Term, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured passenger sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's complaint in equity to compel appellee assignor to assign appellant's claim for benefits to a participating insurer under the Pennsylvania No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act, 40 P. S. § 1009.101 et seq. Appellant contended that he was entitled to benefits as an uninsured.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a Pennsylvania resident, was a passenger in a car that was involved in an accident that resulted in personal injuries to appellant. Appellant and all members of his household were uninsured under the Pennsylvania No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act (Act), P. S. § 1009.101 et seq. The car was not a "secured vehicle" as defined in 40 P. S. § 1009.103. Appellant filed a complaint in equity in the trial court to compel appellee assignor to assign his claim for benefits to a participating insurer under the Act. The trial court dismissed appellant's complaint. The court affirmed. The court stated that it did not agree with appellant that the Assigned Claims Plan could elevate an otherwise uninsured vehicle to secured status. The court stated that § 1009.004 of the Act did not denote the Assigned Claims Plan as an alternative means of securing a vehicle. The court found that omission as evidence of the legislature's intent to consider the procurement of a contract of insurance or qualification as a self-insurer or as an obligated government as the only means of securing a vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which dismissed appellant's complaint in equity to compel appellee assignor to assign appellant's claim for benefits to a participating insurer under a no-fault motor vehicle insurance statute. The court held that the statute could not elevate an otherwise uninsured vehicle to insured status.

Commonwealth v. Sorrell


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


319 Pa. Super. 103; 465 A.2d 1250; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3880


March 11, 1980, Argued  
September 9, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2406 October, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 814-815 February Term, 1978


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant for robbery and conspiracy, alleging the trial court erred in denying appellant's waiver of his right to jury trial, which denial was made pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5104(c), and in refusing to admit appellant's confession because it was made pursuant to an illegal arrest, which was made without probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Following a robbery, an officer, acting on a tip from an informant, the co-defendant's brother, arrested appellant at appellant's home for the robbery. Appellant made no incriminating statements when he was first questioned at the police station, two hours after his arrest. When the questioning resumed, appellant confessed to the robbery. At trial, the trial court denied appellant's request to waive a jury trial, which denial was based on 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5104(c), and refused appellant's motion to suppress his confession. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial and held the trial court erred in refusing to accept appellant's waiver of a jury trial because § 5104(c) was unconstitutional because it contradicted Pa. R. Crim. P. 1101, and in denying appellant's motion to suppress his confession because it was the fruit of the illegal arrest. The court found, considering the totality of the circumstances, the warrantless arrest was not based on probable cause because there was no indication that the arresting officer had any reason to believe the informant was a truthful person nor was the officer informed of the basis for the informant's knowledge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant's motion to suppress, remanded for a new trial, and held the trial court erred in denying appellant's waiver of a jury trial because the statute upon which the denial was based was unconstitutional and in refusing to suppress the confession, which was the product of an illegal arrest, because there was no indication of the informant's veracity or the basis of the informant's knowledge of the robbery.

Commonwealth v. Sherman


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


318 Pa. Super. 440; 465 A.2d 35; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3814

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of burglary, theft, and receiving stolen property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion under Pa. R. Crim. P. 1101 for a bench trial. The commonwealth objected to the waiver and asserted its right to a jury trial pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 5104 (c). The trial court relied upon § 5104 (c) and denied defendant's motion for a bench trial. A jury found defendant guilty of burglary, theft, and receiving stolen property. Superior Court of Pennsylvania


318 Pa. Super. 188; 464 A.2d 1293; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3729


June 16, 1982, Argued  
August 12, 1983, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted November 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 117 Pittsburgh, 1982, Appeal from the Order entered January 19, 1982, Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Civil Action, Law at No. GD 81-00477.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare (department), sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), denying its petition to intervene in a medical malpractice action by plaintiff patient under the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P.S. § 1301.101 et seq., where plaintiff was receiving both Medicaid and cash assistance from the department.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient filed a medical malpractice complaint against defendant doctors under the Health Care Services Malpractice Act (HCSMA), 40 P.S. § 1301.101 et seq. Plaintiff, who was receiving both Medicaid and cash assistance from appellant, the Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare (department), did not notify the department of the pendency of the action. Plaintiff's claim was settled and the trial court ordered the settlement monies placed in escrow. The trial court denied the department's petition to intervene. The court held that 40 P.S. § 1301.602 was inapplicable as no award was involved. The action never reached trial on the facts, therefore, 62 P.S. § 1409(b)(5) permitted the department to become a party to or to consolidate its action with that of plaintiff. Plaintiff was required to notify the department within 30 days of the institution of the action. The department also had the right to become a party to the action and because the department was not given notice of the action, its petition to intervene should have been granted. The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying the petition to intervene of appellant, the Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare (department), and remanded the case for further proceedings. Since plaintiff patient did not give the department 30 days notice of the institution of her medical malpractice action against defendant doctors, its petition to intervene should have been granted. The department had the right to become a party to the action.

Stat. § 903, theft by deception, 18 Pa. Con. Stat. § 3922, criminal mischief, 18 Pa. Con. Stat. § 3304, rigging a publicly exhibited contest, 18 Pa. Con. Stat. § 4109, and perjury, 18 Pa. Con. In re F.L.D.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


317 Pa. Super. 473; 464 A.2d 419; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3591


January 12, 1983, Argued  
July 29, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 680 Pittsburgh 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Washington County, No. 63-80-1264.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County (Pennsylvania), which terminated his parental rights under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2511(a)(1). Appellant asserted that the trial court's decision was not based on clear and convincing evidence, that § 2511(a)(1) was unconstitutionally vague, and that § 2511(a)(1) permitted an unconstitutional state intrusion into the parent-child relationship.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father's parental rights were terminated by the trial court pursuant to 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2511(a)(1). The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. It observed that it appeared that appellant had had no interest in his son at all and would likely not have demanded or attempted to see his son again were it not for the petition at issue. The court concluded that there was clear and convincing evidence to support the trial court's finding that appellant had evidenced a settled purpose to relinquish his parental rights and had failed or refused to perform his parental duties. The court declined to address the issue of whether the words "refused or failed to perform parental duties" contained in § 2511(a)(1) were unconstitutionally vague because it had affirmed the trial court's decision on the basis of settled purpose to relinquish parental rights and that finding alone was sufficient to affirm the trial court's order. The court noted that this was a case where appellant had abdicated his responsibilities as a parent and that the state's limited role in terminating appellant's parental rights was justified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court which terminated appellant father's parental rights. It concluded that there was clear and convincing evidence to support the trial court's finding that appellant had evidenced a settled purpose to relinquish his parental rights and had failed or refused to perform his parental duties.

Greider v. Pennsylvania Assigned Claims Plan


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


316 Pa. Super. 146; 462 A.2d 836; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3447


February 3, 1983, Argued  
July 8, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 3310 Philadelphia, 1981, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County, No. 1008 August Term, 1979.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an injured child and her mother, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment to appellee state insurance plan in appellants' action for basic loss benefits where appellant child was injured in an automobile accident in another state and was neither an insured nor an occupant of a secured vehicle.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, an injured child and her mother, filed a claim for basic loss benefits with appellee state insurance plan. Appellee denied that claim pursuant to the Pennsylvania No-Fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act (Act), 40 P.S. § 1009.201, on the grounds that the accident in question occurred out-of-state in an uninsured vehicle, and that appellants did not own a vehicle or an insurance policy themselves. Appellants filed suit, and the trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that § 1009.201 was not unconstitutional on equal protection or due process grounds, because the legislature's limiting of the Act to accidents which occurred in the state and/or accidents in which the victim or vehicle was insured was reasonable and rationally related to the legitimate purposes of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to appellee state insurance plan in the suit for basic loss benefits brought by appellants, an injured child and her mother, because the Pennsylvania No-Fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act's limitation of such benefits to in-state accidents or to out-of-state accidents involving only insureds was rationally related to a legitimate state purpose and thus was constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Doe


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


316 Pa. Super. 1; 462 A.2d 762; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3361


December 13, 1982, Submitted  
June 24, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2510 Philadelphia 1981, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of August 27, 1981, Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County, Criminal Division, at No. 1947A of 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sough review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which found her guilty of violating the obscenity statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5903(a)(2), sentenced her to three years probation, and assessed a fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating the obscenity statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5903, after two detectives bought magazines depicting various sexual acts and viewed "peep shows" while in a store where defendant was the only employee. Defendant was fined and sentenced to three years probation and she subsequently challenged her conviction. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the court found that defendant did not preserve her complaints regarding voir dire because she failed to raise objections at the time of the voir dire. Furthermore, the court held that a district attorney's testimony that defendant had stated in an unrelated suppression hearing that she was the manager of the store was properly admitted because it was relevant to prove that defendant knew or should have known that the material sold in the store was obscene. The court also found there was sufficient evidence to support the statute's element of knowledge that the material being sold was obscene and that § 5903 was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, convicting defendant of violating the obscenity statute, because there was sufficient evidence to support a finding that defendant knew our should have known that the material being sold in the store was obscene. Furthermore, the court held that a district attorney's testimony that defendant had previously stated that she was the store manager was relevant to prove the element of knowledge.

 

525 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re McMullins


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


315 Pa. Super. 531; 462 A.2d 718; 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3289


February 1, 1983, Argued  
June 17, 1983, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1567 Philadelphia, 1982, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Luzerne County, No. 1576-C of 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which, after appellant was adjudicated a delinquent, ordered him committed for inpatient psychiatric treatment in the forensic adolescent unit at a state hospital for a period not to exceed 90 days.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile was adjudicated a delinquent. He was found guilty of simple assault, indecent assault, and making terroristic threats. The juvenile probation officer filed a petition for commitment of 90 days pursuant to the Mental Health Procedures Act (act), 50 P.S. § 7406. After an evidentiary hearing the mental health review officer found appellant in need of psychiatric treatment and recommended the petition be granted. The lower court ordered him committed for inpatient psychiatric treatment in the forensic adolescent unit at a state hospital. The issue before the court was one of first impression, whether § 7406 applied to juveniles who had been adjudicated delinquent. The court found that 50 P.S. § 7401(c) referenced the consideration of a child's age in the application of the act's provisions; nowhere else in the statute was there a distinction between adults and juveniles with regard to involuntary examination and treatment. The act gave trial courts broad authority to order examination to aid it in sentencing, or to order involuntary treatment in aid of sentencing, or when the person is severely mentally disabled and in need of treatment. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order because the lower court had authority to order a mental examination of an adjudicated delinquent to aid it in its final disposition and to order involuntary treatment. The Mental Health Procedures Act authorizing such actions by the lower courts did not distinguish between adults and juveniles.

COMMONWEALTH v. GARWOOD


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3086
